<p><b>Alexander III of Macedon</b> (20/21 July 356 BC – 10/11 June 323 BC), commonly known as <b>Alexander the Great</b> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el" xml:lang="el">Ἀλέξανδρος ὁ Μέγας</span>, <i>Aléxandros ho Mégas</i> <small>Koine Greek: </small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Greek" title="Help:IPA for Greek">[a.lék.san.dros ho mé.gas]</a></span> ), was a king (<i><a href="/wiki/Basileus" title="Basileus">basileus</a></i>) of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greek</a> kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedon</a><sup class="reference" id="ref_a_1"><a href="#cnote_a">[a]</a></sup> and a member of the <a href="/wiki/Argead_dynasty" title="Argead dynasty">Argead dynasty</a>. Born in <a href="/wiki/Pella" title="Pella">Pella</a> in 356 BC, Alexander succeeded his father, <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II</a>, to the throne at the age of twenty. He spent most of his ruling years on an unprecedented military campaign through Asia and northeast Africa, and by the age of thirty he had created one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_empires" title="List of largest empires">largest empires</a> of the ancient world, stretching from <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> to northwestern <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> He was undefeated in battle and is widely considered one of history's most successful military commanders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYenne2010159_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYenne2010159-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>During his youth, Alexander was tutored by the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> until the age of 16. After Philip's assassination in 336 BC, Alexander succeeded his father to the throne and inherited a strong kingdom and an experienced army. Alexander was awarded the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Corinth" title="League of Corinth">generalship of Greece</a> and used this authority to launch his father's Panhellenic project to lead the Greeks in the conquest of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> In 334 BC, he invaded the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> (Persian Empire), and began a <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Wars of Alexander the Great">series of campaigns</a> that lasted ten years. Following the conquest of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, Alexander broke the power of Persia in a series of decisive battles, most notably the battles of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Issus" title="Battle of Issus">Issus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gaugamela" title="Battle of Gaugamela">Gaugamela</a>. He subsequently overthrew the Persian King <a href="/wiki/Darius_III" title="Darius III">Darius III</a> and conquered the Achaemenid Empire in its entirety.<sup class="reference" id="ref_b_1"><a href="#cnote_b">[b]</a></sup> At that point, his empire stretched from the <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Sea" title="Adriatic Sea">Adriatic Sea</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus River</a>.</p>
<p>Seeking to reach the "ends of the world and the Great Outer Sea", he <a href="/wiki/Indian_campaign_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Indian campaign of Alexander the Great">invaded India</a> in 326 BC, but eventually turned back at the demand of his homesick troops. Alexander died in <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> in 323 BC, the city he planned to establish as his capital, without executing a series of planned campaigns that would have begun with an invasion of <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabia</a>. In the years following his death, a <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Diadochi" title="Wars of the Diadochi">series of civil wars</a> tore his empire apart, resulting in several states ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Diadochi" title="Diadochi">Diadochi</a>, Alexander's surviving generals and heirs.</p>
<p>Alexander's legacy includes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultural_diffusion" title="Cultural diffusion">cultural diffusion</a> his conquests engendered, such as <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhism" title="Greco-Buddhism">Greco-Buddhism</a>. He founded some <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Alexandria_(disambiguation)#Other_places_founded_or_renamed_Alexandria_by_Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexandria (disambiguation)">twenty cities that bore his name</a>, most notably <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> in Egypt. Alexander's settlement of Greek colonists and the resulting spread of <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Greece" title="Culture of Greece">Greek culture</a> in the east resulted in a new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" title="Hellenistic civilization">Hellenistic civilization</a>, aspects of which were still evident in the traditions of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> in the mid-15th century and the presence of <a href="/wiki/Cappadocian_Greeks" title="Cappadocian Greeks">Greek speakers in central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Greeks" title="Pontic Greeks">far eastern Anatolia</a> until the 1920s. Alexander became legendary as a classical hero in the mold of <a href="/wiki/Achilles" title="Achilles">Achilles</a>, and he features prominently in the history and mythic traditions of both Greek and non-Greek cultures. He became the measure against which military leaders compared themselves, and <a href="/wiki/Military_academy" title="Military academy">military academies</a> throughout the world still teach his tactics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYenne2010viii_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYenne2010viii-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="ref_c_1"><a href="#cnote_c">[c]</a></sup> He is often ranked among the most influential people in human history, along with his teacher Aristotle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>

<ul>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Early_life"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Early life</span></a>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lineage_and_childhood">Lineage and childhood</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AlexanderTheGreat_Bust.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1062" data-file-width="768" height="304" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/AlexanderTheGreat_Bust.jpg/220px-AlexanderTheGreat_Bust.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/AlexanderTheGreat_Bust.jpg/330px-AlexanderTheGreat_Bust.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/AlexanderTheGreat_Bust.jpg/440px-AlexanderTheGreat_Bust.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AlexanderTheGreat_Bust.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bust of a young Alexander the Great from the Hellenistic era, <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle_tutoring_Alexander.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="578" data-file-width="403" height="316" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Aristotle_tutoring_Alexander.jpg/220px-Aristotle_tutoring_Alexander.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Aristotle_tutoring_Alexander.jpg/330px-Aristotle_tutoring_Alexander.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f2/Aristotle_tutoring_Alexander.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle_tutoring_Alexander.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> tutoring Alexander</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Jean_Leon_Gerome_Ferris" title="Jean Leon Gerome Ferris">Jean Leon Gerome Ferris</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alexander was born on the sixth day of the ancient Greek month of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hekatombaion" title="Hekatombaion">Hekatombaion</a>, which probably corresponds to 20<span class="nowrap"> </span>July 356 BC, although the exact date is disputed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Pella" title="Pella">Pella</a>, the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Kingdom of Macedon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> He was the son of the king of Macedon, <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II</a>, and his fourth wife, <a href="/wiki/Olympias" title="Olympias">Olympias</a>, the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Neoptolemus_I_of_Epirus" title="Neoptolemus I of Epirus">Neoptolemus I</a>, king of <a href="/wiki/Epirus_(ancient_state)" title="Epirus (ancient state)">Epirus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Although Philip had seven or eight wives, Olympias was his principal wife for some time, likely a result of giving birth to Alexander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010171_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010171-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several legends surround Alexander's birth and childhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010188_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010188-12">[12]</a></sup> According to the ancient Greek biographer <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>, Olympias, on the eve of the consummation of her marriage to Philip, dreamed that her womb was struck by a thunder bolt, causing a flame that spread "far and wide" before dying away. Sometime after the wedding, Philip is said to have seen himself, in a dream, securing his wife's womb with a <a href="/wiki/Seal_(emblem)" title="Seal (emblem)">seal</a> engraved with a lion's image.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA2_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA2-13">[13]</a></sup> Plutarch offered a variety of interpretations of these dreams: that Olympias was pregnant before her marriage, indicated by the sealing of her womb; or that Alexander's father was <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>. Ancient commentators were divided about whether the ambitious Olympias promulgated the story of Alexander's divine parentage, variously claiming that she had told Alexander, or that she dismissed the suggestion as impious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA2_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-PA2-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the day Alexander was born, Philip was preparing a <a href="/wiki/Siege" title="Siege">siege</a> on the city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potidea" title="Potidea">Potidea</a> on the peninsula of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chalcidice" title="Chalcidice">Chalcidice</a>. That same day, Philip received news that his general <a href="/wiki/Parmenion" title="Parmenion">Parmenion</a> had defeated the combined <a href="/wiki/Illyria" title="Illyria">Illyrian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paeonian" title="Paeonian">Paeonian</a> armies, and that his horses had won at the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Olympic_Games" title="Ancient Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a>. It was also said that on this day, the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Artemis" title="Temple of Artemis">Temple of Artemis</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Wonders_of_the_Ancient_World" title="Seven Wonders of the Ancient World">Seven Wonders of the World</a>, burnt down. This led <a href="/wiki/Hegesias_of_Magnesia" title="Hegesias of Magnesia">Hegesias of Magnesia</a> to say that it had burnt down because <a href="/wiki/Artemis" title="Artemis">Artemis</a> was away, attending the birth of Alexander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Such legends may have emerged when Alexander was king, and possibly at his own instigation, to show that he was superhuman and destined for greatness from conception.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010188_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010188-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his early years, Alexander was raised by a nurse, <a href="/wiki/Lanike" title="Lanike">Lanike</a>, sister of Alexander's future general <a href="/wiki/Cleitus_the_Black" title="Cleitus the Black">Cleitus the Black</a>. Later in his childhood, Alexander was tutored by the strict <a href="/wiki/Leonidas_of_Epirus" title="Leonidas of Epirus">Leonidas</a>, a relative of his mother, and by <a href="/wiki/Lysimachus_of_Acarnania" title="Lysimachus of Acarnania">Lysimachus of Acarnania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERenault200133.E2.80.9334_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERenault200133.E2.80.9334-15">[15]</a></sup> Alexander was raised in the manner of noble Macedonian youths, learning to read, play the <a href="/wiki/Lyre" title="Lyre">lyre</a>, ride, fight, and hunt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>When Alexander was ten years old, a trader from <a href="/wiki/Thessaly" title="Thessaly">Thessaly</a> brought Philip a horse, which he offered to sell for thirteen <a href="/wiki/Attic_talent" title="Attic talent">talents</a>. The horse refused to be mounted and Philip ordered it away. Alexander however, detecting the horse's fear of its own shadow, asked to tame the horse, which he eventually managed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010188_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010188-12">[12]</a></sup> Plutarch stated that Philip, overjoyed at this display of courage and ambition, kissed his son tearfully, declaring: "My boy, you must find a kingdom big enough for your ambitions. Macedon is too small for you", and bought the horse for him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA6_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA6-17">[17]</a></sup> Alexander named it <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bucephalas" title="Bucephalas">Bucephalas</a>, meaning "ox-head". Bucephalas carried Alexander as far as <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>. When the animal died (due to old age, according to Plutarch, at age thirty), Alexander named a city after him, <a href="/wiki/Alexandria_Bucephalous" title="Alexandria Bucephalous">Bucephala</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adolescence_and_education">Adolescence and education</span></h3>
<p>When Alexander was 13, Philip began to search for a <a href="/wiki/Tutor" title="Tutor">tutor</a>, and considered such academics as <a href="/wiki/Isocrates" title="Isocrates">Isocrates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Speusippus" title="Speusippus">Speusippus</a>, the latter offering to resign to take up the post. In the end, Philip chose <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> and provided the Temple of the Nymphs at <a href="/wiki/Mieza,_Macedonia" title="Mieza, Macedonia">Mieza</a> as a classroom. In return for teaching Alexander, Philip agreed to rebuild Aristotle's hometown of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stageira" title="Stageira">Stageira</a>, which Philip had razed, and to repopulate it by buying and freeing the ex-citizens who were slaves, or pardoning those who were in exile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mieza was like a boarding school for Alexander and the children of Macedonian nobles, such as <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_I_Soter" title="Ptolemy I Soter">Ptolemy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hephaistion" title="Hephaistion">Hephaistion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cassander" title="Cassander">Cassander</a>. Many of these students would become his friends and future generals, and are often known as the 'Companions'. Aristotle taught Alexander and his companions about medicine, philosophy, morals, religion, logic, and art. Under Aristotle's tutelage, Alexander developed a passion for the works of <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>, and in particular the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i>; Aristotle gave him an annotated copy, which Alexander later carried on his campaigns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philip.27s_heir">Philip's heir</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regency_and_ascent_of_Macedon">Regency and ascent of Macedon</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II of Macedon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rise_of_Macedon" title="Rise of Macedon">Rise of Macedon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Filip_II_Macedonia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1466" data-file-width="1016" height="317" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Filip_II_Macedonia.jpg/220px-Filip_II_Macedonia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Filip_II_Macedonia.jpg/330px-Filip_II_Macedonia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Filip_II_Macedonia.jpg/440px-Filip_II_Macedonia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Filip_II_Macedonia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II of Macedon</a>, Alexander's father.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At age 16, Alexander's education under Aristotle ended. Philip waged war against <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantion</a>, leaving Alexander in charge as <a href="/wiki/Regent" title="Regent">regent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heir_apparent" title="Heir apparent">heir apparent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010188_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010188-12">[12]</a></sup> During Philip's absence, the <a href="/wiki/Thracians" title="Thracians">Thracian</a> <a href="/wiki/Maedi" title="Maedi">Maedi</a> revolted against Macedonia. Alexander responded quickly, driving them from their territory. He colonized it with Greeks, and founded a city named <a href="/wiki/Alexandropolis_Maedica" title="Alexandropolis Maedica">Alexandropolis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Upon Philip's return, he dispatched Alexander with a small force to subdue revolts in southern <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a>. Campaigning against the Greek city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perinthus" title="Perinthus">Perinthus</a>, Alexander is reported to have saved his father's life. Meanwhile, the city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphissa_(city)" title="Amphissa (city)">Amphissa</a> began to work lands that were sacred to <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> near <a href="/wiki/Delphi" title="Delphi">Delphi</a>, a sacrilege that gave Philip the opportunity to further intervene in Greek affairs. Still occupied in Thrace, he ordered Alexander to muster an army for a campaign in southern Greece. Concerned that other Greek states might intervene, Alexander made it look as though he was preparing to attack Illyria instead. During this turmoil, the Illyrians invaded Macedonia, only to be repelled by Alexander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERenault200147.E2.80.9349_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERenault200147.E2.80.9349-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Philip and his army joined his son in 338 BC, and they marched south through <a href="/wiki/Thermopylae" title="Thermopylae">Thermopylae</a>, taking it after stubborn resistance from its Theban garrison. They went on to occupy the city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elatea" title="Elatea">Elatea</a>, only a few days' march from both <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">Thebes</a>. The Athenians, led by <a href="/wiki/Demosthenes" title="Demosthenes">Demosthenes</a>, voted to seek alliance with Thebes against Macedonia. Both Athens and Philip sent embassies to win Thebes' favour, but Athens won the contest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Philip marched on Amphissa (ostensibly acting on the request of the <a href="/wiki/Amphictyonic_League" title="Amphictyonic League">Amphictyonic League</a>), capturing the mercenaries sent there by Demosthenes and accepting the city's surrender. Philip then returned to Elatea, sending a final offer of peace to Athens and Thebes, who both rejected it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander1256.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="441" height="399" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Alexander1256.jpg/220px-Alexander1256.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Alexander1256.jpg/330px-Alexander1256.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Alexander1256.jpg/440px-Alexander1256.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander1256.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Statue of Alexander in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Istanbul_Archaeology_Museum" title="Istanbul Archaeology Museum">Istanbul Archaeology Museum</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As Philip marched south, his opponents blocked him near <a href="/wiki/Chaeronea" title="Chaeronea">Chaeronea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boeotia" title="Boeotia">Boeotia</a>. During the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chaeronea_(338_BC)" title="Battle of Chaeronea (338 BC)">Battle of Chaeronea</a>, Philip commanded the right wing and Alexander the left, accompanied by a group of Philip's trusted generals. According to the ancient sources, the two sides fought bitterly for some time. Philip deliberately commanded his troops to retreat, counting on the untested Athenian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hoplites" title="Hoplites">hoplites</a> to follow, thus breaking their line. Alexander was the first to break the Theban lines, followed by Philip's generals. Having damaged the enemy's cohesion, Philip ordered his troops to press forward and quickly routed them. With the Athenians lost, the Thebans were surrounded. Left to fight alone, they were defeated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DiodXVI_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-DiodXVI-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the victory at Chaeronea, Philip and Alexander marched unopposed into the Peloponnese, welcomed by all cities; however, when they reached <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>, they were refused, but did not resort to war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Corinth" title="Ancient Corinth">Corinth</a>, Philip established a "Hellenic Alliance" (modelled on the old <a href="/wiki/Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece#Hellenic_alliance" title="Second Persian invasion of Greece">anti-Persian alliance</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">Greco-Persian Wars</a>), which included most Greek city-states except Sparta. Philip was then named <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hegemon" title="Hegemon">Hegemon</a></i> (often translated as "Supreme Commander") of this league (known by modern scholars as the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Corinth" title="League of Corinth">League of Corinth</a>), and announced his plans to attack the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Persian Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERenault200154_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERenault200154-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarty200426_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarty200426-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exile_and_return">Exile and return</span></h3>
<p>When Philip returned to Pella, he fell in love with and married <a href="/wiki/Cleopatra_Eurydice_of_Macedon" title="Cleopatra Eurydice of Macedon">Cleopatra Eurydice</a>, the niece of his general <a href="/wiki/Attalus_(general)" title="Attalus (general)">Attalus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010179_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010179-29">[29]</a></sup> The marriage made Alexander's position as heir less secure, since any son of Cleopatra Eurydice would be a fully Macedonian heir, while Alexander was only half-Macedonian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarty200427_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarty200427-30">[30]</a></sup> During the wedding banquet, a drunken Attalus publicly prayed to the gods that the union would produce a legitimate heir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010179_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010179-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>At the wedding of Cleopatra, whom Philip fell in love with and married, she being much too young for him, her uncle Attalus in his drink desired the Macedonians would implore the gods to give them a lawful successor to the kingdom by his niece. This so irritated Alexander, that throwing one of the cups at his head, "You villain," said he, "what, am I then a bastard?" Then Philip, taking Attalus's part, rose up and would have run his son through; but by good fortune for them both, either his over-hasty rage, or the wine he had drunk, made his foot slip, so that he fell down on the floor. At which Alexander reproachfully insulted over him: "See there," said he, "the man who makes preparations to pass out of Europe into Asia, overturned in passing from one seat to another."</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Plutarch, describing the feud at Philip's wedding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA9_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA9-31">[31]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Alexander fled Macedon with his mother, dropping her off with her brother, King <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Epirus" title="Alexander I of Epirus">Alexander I of Epirus</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dodona" title="Dodona">Dodona</a>, capital of the <a href="/wiki/Molossians" title="Molossians">Molossians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180-32">[32]</a></sup> He continued to Illyria,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180-32">[32]</a></sup> where he sought refuge with the Illyrian king and was treated as a guest, despite having defeated them in battle a few years before. However, it appears Philip never intended to disown his politically and militarily trained son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180-32">[32]</a></sup> Accordingly, Alexander returned to Macedon after six months due to the efforts of a family friend, <a href="/wiki/Demaratus_(hetairos)" title="Demaratus (hetairos)">Demaratus</a>, who mediated between the two parties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the following year, the Persian <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrap</a> (governor) of <a href="/wiki/Caria" title="Caria">Caria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pixodarus_of_Caria" title="Pixodarus of Caria">Pixodarus</a>, offered his eldest daughter to Alexander's half-brother, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philip_Arrhidaeus" title="Philip Arrhidaeus">Philip Arrhidaeus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180-32">[32]</a></sup> Olympias and several of Alexander's friends suggested this showed Philip intended to make Arrhidaeus his heir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180_32-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180-32">[32]</a></sup> Alexander reacted by sending an actor, <a href="/wiki/Thessalus_(actor)" title="Thessalus (actor)">Thessalus</a> of Corinth, to tell Pixodarus that he should not offer his daughter's hand to an illegitimate son, but instead to Alexander. When Philip heard of this, he stopped the negotiations and scolded Alexander for wishing to marry the daughter of a Carian, explaining that he wanted a better bride for him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180_32-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180-32">[32]</a></sup> Philip exiled four of Alexander's friends, <a href="/wiki/Harpalus" title="Harpalus">Harpalus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nearchus" title="Nearchus">Nearchus</a>, Ptolemy and <a href="/wiki/Erigyius" title="Erigyius">Erigyius</a>, and had the Corinthians bring Thessalus to him in chains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="King_of_Macedon">King of Macedon</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accession">Accession</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_Macedonia_336_BC-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="794" data-file-width="992" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Map_Macedonia_336_BC-en.svg/220px-Map_Macedonia_336_BC-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Map_Macedonia_336_BC-en.svg/330px-Map_Macedonia_336_BC-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Map_Macedonia_336_BC-en.svg/440px-Map_Macedonia_336_BC-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_Macedonia_336_BC-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Kingdom of Macedon in 336 BC.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In summer 336 BC, while at <a href="/wiki/Vergina" title="Vergina">Aegae</a> attending the wedding of his daughter <a href="/wiki/Cleopatra_of_Macedon" title="Cleopatra of Macedon">Cleopatra</a> to Olympias's brother, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Epirus" title="Alexander I of Epirus">Alexander I of Epirus</a>, Philip was assassinated by the captain of his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Somatophylax" title="Somatophylax">bodyguards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_of_Orestis" title="Pausanias of Orestis">Pausanias</a>.<sup class="reference" id="ref_e_1"><a href="#cnote_e">[e]</a></sup> As Pausanias tried to escape, he tripped over a vine and was killed by his pursuers, including two of Alexander's companions, <a href="/wiki/Perdiccas" title="Perdiccas">Perdiccas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leonnatus" title="Leonnatus">Leonnatus</a>. Alexander was proclaimed king on the spot by the nobles and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Macedonian_military" title="Ancient Macedonian military">army</a> at the age of 20.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarty200430.E2.80.9331_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarty200430.E2.80.9331-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fox_1980_72_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fox_1980_72-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consolidation_of_power">Consolidation of power</span></h3>
<p>Alexander began his reign by eliminating potential rivals to the throne. He had his cousin, the former <a href="/wiki/Amyntas_IV_of_Macedon" title="Amyntas IV of Macedon">Amyntas IV</a>, executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010190_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010190-38">[38]</a></sup> He also had two Macedonian princes from the region of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyncestis" title="Lyncestis">Lyncestis</a> killed, but spared a third, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_Lyncestes" title="Alexander Lyncestes">Alexander Lyncestes</a>. Olympias had Cleopatra Eurydice and Europa, her daughter by Philip, burned alive. When Alexander learned about this, he was furious. Alexander also ordered the murder of Attalus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010190_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010190-38">[38]</a></sup> who was in command of the advance guard of the army in Asia Minor and Cleopatra's uncle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green_2007_5.E2.80.936_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Green_2007_5.E2.80.936-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Attalus was at that time corresponding with Demosthenes, regarding the possibility of defecting to Athens. Attalus also had severely insulted Alexander, and following Cleopatra's murder, Alexander may have considered him too dangerous to leave alive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green_2007_5.E2.80.936_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Green_2007_5.E2.80.936-39">[39]</a></sup> Alexander spared Arrhidaeus, who was by all accounts mentally disabled, possibly as a result of poisoning by Olympias.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarty200430.E2.80.9331_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarty200430.E2.80.9331-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fox_1980_72_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fox_1980_72-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>News of Philip's death roused many states into revolt, including Thebes, Athens, Thessaly, and the Thracian tribes north of Macedon. When news of the revolts reached Alexander, he responded quickly. Though advised to use diplomacy, Alexander mustered 3,000 Macedonian cavalry and rode south towards Thessaly. He found the Thessalian army occupying the pass between <a href="/wiki/Mount_Olympus" title="Mount Olympus">Mount Olympus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Ossa_(Greece)" title="Mount Ossa (Greece)">Mount Ossa</a>, and ordered his men to ride over Mount Ossa. When the Thessalians awoke the next day, they found Alexander in their rear and promptly surrendered, adding their cavalry to Alexander's force. He then continued south towards the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnese</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Alexander stopped at Thermopylae, where he was recognized as the leader of the Amphictyonic League before heading south to <a href="/wiki/Corinth" title="Corinth">Corinth</a>. Athens sued for peace and Alexander pardoned the rebels. The famous <a href="/wiki/Diogenes_and_Alexander" title="Diogenes and Alexander">encounter between Alexander and Diogenes the Cynic</a> occurred during Alexander's stay in Corinth. When Alexander asked Diogenes what he could do for him, the philosopher disdainfully asked Alexander to stand a little to the side, as he was blocking the sunlight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoneman200421_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoneman200421-42">[42]</a></sup> This reply apparently delighted Alexander, who is reported to have said "But verily, if I were not Alexander, I would like to be Diogenes."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDillon2004187.E2.80.93188_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDillon2004187.E2.80.93188-43">[43]</a></sup> At Corinth, Alexander took the title of <i>Hegemon</i> ("leader") and, like Philip, was appointed commander for the coming war against Persia. He also received news of a Thracian uprising.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Balkan_campaign">Balkan campaign</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alexander%27s_Balkan_campaign" title="Alexander's Balkan campaign">Alexander's Balkan campaign</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deer_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="462" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Deer_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg/220px-Deer_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Deer_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg/330px-Deer_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Deer_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg/440px-Deer_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Deer_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The emblema of the <a href="/wiki/Stag_Hunt_Mosaic" title="Stag Hunt Mosaic">Stag Hunt Mosaic</a>, c. 300 BC, from <a href="/wiki/Pella" title="Pella">Pella</a>; the figure on the right is possibly Alexander the Great due to the date of the mosaic along with the depicted upsweep of his centrally-parted hair (<i>anastole</i>); the figure on the left wielding a double-edged axe (associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hephaistos" title="Hephaistos">Hephaistos</a>) is perhaps <a href="/wiki/Hephaestion" title="Hephaestion">Hephaestion</a>, one of Alexander's loyal companions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bare_url_a_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-bare_url_a-45">[45]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Before crossing to Asia, Alexander wanted to safeguard his northern borders. In the spring of 335 BC, he advanced to suppress several revolts. Starting from <a href="/wiki/Amphipolis" title="Amphipolis">Amphipolis</a>, he travelled east into the country of the "Independent Thracians"; and at <a href="/wiki/Haemus_Mons" title="Haemus Mons">Mount Haemus</a>, the Macedonian army attacked and defeated the Thracian forces manning the heights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-I.2C_1_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-I.2C_1-46">[46]</a></sup> The Macedonians marched into the country of the <a href="/wiki/Triballi" title="Triballi">Triballi</a>, and defeated their army near the Lyginus river<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> (a <a href="/wiki/List_of_tributaries_of_the_Danube" title="List of tributaries of the Danube">tributary of the Danube</a>). Alexander then marched for three days to the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a>, encountering the <a href="/wiki/Getae" title="Getae">Getae</a> tribe on the opposite shore. Crossing the river at night, he surprised them and forced their army to retreat after the first cavalry <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skirmish" title="Skirmish">skirmish</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>News then reached Alexander that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleitus_the_Illyrian" title="Cleitus the Illyrian">Cleitus</a>, King of Illyria, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Glaukias" title="King Glaukias">King Glaukias</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taulanti" title="Taulanti">Taulanti</a> were in open revolt against his authority. Marching west into Illyria, Alexander defeated each in turn, forcing the two rulers to flee with their troops. With these victories, he secured his northern frontier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>While Alexander campaigned north, the Thebans and Athenians rebelled once again. Alexander immediately headed south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192-50">[50]</a></sup> While the other cities again hesitated, Thebes decided to fight. The Theban resistance was ineffective, and Alexander razed the city and divided its territory between the other Boeotian cities. The end of Thebes cowed Athens, leaving all of Greece temporarily at peace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192-50">[50]</a></sup> Alexander then set out on his Asian campaign, leaving <a href="/wiki/Antipater" title="Antipater">Antipater</a> as regent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_199_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_199-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conquest_of_the_Persian_Empire">Conquest of the Persian Empire</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Wars of Alexander the Great">Wars of Alexander the Great</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_the_expedition_of_Alexander_the_Great_into_Asia" title="Chronology of the expedition of Alexander the Great into Asia">Chronology of the expedition of Alexander the Great into Asia</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia_Minor">Asia Minor</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Granicus" title="Battle of the Granicus">Battle of the Granicus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Halicarnassus" title="Siege of Halicarnassus">Siege of Halicarnassus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Miletus" title="Siege of Miletus">Siege of Miletus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MacedonEmpire.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="951" data-file-width="1990" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/MacedonEmpire.jpg/350px-MacedonEmpire.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/MacedonEmpire.jpg/525px-MacedonEmpire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/MacedonEmpire.jpg/700px-MacedonEmpire.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MacedonEmpire.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of Alexander's empire and his route</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alexander's army crossed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellespont" title="Hellespont">Hellespont</a> in 334 BC with approximately 48,100 soldiers, 6,100 cavalry and a fleet of 120 ships with crews numbering 38,000,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192-50">[50]</a></sup> drawn from Macedon and various Greek city-states, mercenaries, and feudally raised soldiers from <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paionia" title="Paionia">Paionia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Illyria" title="Illyria">Illyria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="ref_f_1"><a href="#cnote_f">[f]</a></sup> He showed his intent to conquer the entirety of the Persian Empire by throwing a spear into Asian soil and saying he accepted Asia as a gift from the gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192_50-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192-50">[50]</a></sup> This also showed Alexander's eagerness to fight, in contrast to his father's preference for diplomacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192_50-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>After an initial victory against Persian forces at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Granicus" title="Battle of the Granicus">Battle of the Granicus</a>, Alexander accepted the surrender of the Persian provincial capital and treasury of <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardis</a>; he then proceeded along the <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionian</a> coast, granting autonomy and democracy to the cities. Miletus, held by Achaemenid forces, required a delicate siege operation, with Persian naval forces nearby. Further south, at <a href="/wiki/Halicarnassus" title="Halicarnassus">Halicarnassus</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Caria" title="Caria">Caria</a>, Alexander successfully waged his first large-scale <a href="/wiki/Siege" title="Siege">siege</a>, eventually forcing his opponents, the mercenary captain <a href="/wiki/Memnon_of_Rhodes" title="Memnon of Rhodes">Memnon of Rhodes</a> and the Persian <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrap</a> of Caria, <a href="/wiki/Orontobates" title="Orontobates">Orontobates</a>, to withdraw by sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Alexander left the government of Caria to a member of the Hecatomnid dynasty, <a href="/wiki/Ada_of_Caria" title="Ada of Caria">Ada</a>, who adopted Alexander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arrian_1976_loc.3DI.2C_23_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arrian_1976_loc.3DI.2C_23-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>From Halicarnassus, Alexander proceeded into mountainous <a href="/wiki/Lycia" title="Lycia">Lycia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pamphylia" title="Pamphylia">Pamphylian</a> plain, asserting control over all coastal cities to deny the Persians naval bases. From Pamphylia onwards the coast held no major ports and Alexander moved inland. At <a href="/wiki/Termessos" title="Termessos">Termessos</a>, Alexander humbled but did not storm the <a href="/wiki/Pisidia" title="Pisidia">Pisidian</a> city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> At the ancient Phrygian capital of <a href="/wiki/Gordium" title="Gordium">Gordium</a>, Alexander "undid" the hitherto unsolvable <a href="/wiki/Gordian_Knot" title="Gordian Knot">Gordian Knot</a>, a feat said to await the future "king of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> According to the story, Alexander proclaimed that it did not matter how the knot was undone and hacked it apart with his sword.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Levant_and_Syria">The Levant and Syria</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Napoli_BW_2013-05-16_16-25-06_1_DxO.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2133" data-file-width="3562" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Napoli_BW_2013-05-16_16-25-06_1_DxO.jpg/220px-Napoli_BW_2013-05-16_16-25-06_1_DxO.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Napoli_BW_2013-05-16_16-25-06_1_DxO.jpg/330px-Napoli_BW_2013-05-16_16-25-06_1_DxO.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Napoli_BW_2013-05-16_16-25-06_1_DxO.jpg/440px-Napoli_BW_2013-05-16_16-25-06_1_DxO.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Napoli_BW_2013-05-16_16-25-06_1_DxO.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Detail of Alexander Mosaic, showing <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Issus" title="Battle of Issus">Battle of Issus</a>, from the <a href="/wiki/House_of_the_Faun" title="House of the Faun">House of the Faun</a>, Pompeii.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Issus" title="Battle of Issus">Battle of Issus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Tyre_(332_BC)" title="Siege of Tyre (332 BC)">Siege of Tyre (332 BC)</a></div>
<p>In spring 333 BC, Alexander crossed the Taurus into Cilicia. After a long pause due to illness, he marched on towards Syria. Though outmanoeuvered by Darius' significantly larger army, he marched back to Cilicia, where he defeated Darius at Issus. Darius fled the battle, causing his army to collapse, and left behind his wife, his two daughters, his mother <a href="/wiki/Sisygambis" title="Sisygambis">Sisygambis</a>, and a fabulous treasure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> He offered a <a href="/wiki/Peace_treaty" title="Peace treaty">peace treaty</a> that included the lands he had already lost, and a ransom of 10,000 <a href="/wiki/Attic_talent" title="Attic talent">talents</a> for his family. Alexander replied that since he was now king of Asia, it was he alone who decided territorial divisions.</p>
<p>Alexander proceeded to take possession of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Syria" title="Ancient Syria">Syria</a>, and most of the coast of the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arrian_1976_loc.3DI.2C_23_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Arrian_1976_loc.3DI.2C_23-54">[54]</a></sup> In the following year, 332 BC, he was forced to attack <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">Tyre</a>, which he captured after a long and difficult <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Tyre_(332_BC)" title="Siege of Tyre (332 BC)">siege</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The men of military age were massacred and the women and children sold into <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Egypt">Egypt</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Gaza" title="Siege of Gaza">Siege of Gaza</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Name_of_Alexander_the_Great_in_Hieroglyphs_circa_330_BCE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1964" data-file-width="2965" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Name_of_Alexander_the_Great_in_Hieroglyphs_circa_330_BCE.jpg/220px-Name_of_Alexander_the_Great_in_Hieroglyphs_circa_330_BCE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Name_of_Alexander_the_Great_in_Hieroglyphs_circa_330_BCE.jpg/330px-Name_of_Alexander_the_Great_in_Hieroglyphs_circa_330_BCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Name_of_Alexander_the_Great_in_Hieroglyphs_circa_330_BCE.jpg/440px-Name_of_Alexander_the_Great_in_Hieroglyphs_circa_330_BCE.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Name_of_Alexander_the_Great_in_Hieroglyphs_circa_330_BCE.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Name of Alexander the Great in <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_hieroglyphs" title="Egyptian hieroglyphs">Egyptian hieroglyphs</a> (written from right to left), c. 330 BC, Egypt. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louvre_Museum" title="Louvre Museum">Louvre Museum</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When Alexander destroyed Tyre, most of the towns on the route to <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> quickly capitulated. However, Alexander met with resistance at <a href="/wiki/Gaza_City" title="Gaza City">Gaza</a>. The stronghold was heavily fortified and built on a hill, requiring a siege. When "his engineers pointed out to him that because of the height of the mound it would be impossible… this encouraged Alexander all the more to make the attempt".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> After three unsuccessful assaults, the stronghold fell, but not before Alexander had received a serious shoulder wound. As in Tyre, men of military age were put to the sword and the women and children were sold into slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>Alexander advanced on Egypt in later 332 BC, where he was regarded as a liberator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> He was pronounced son of the deity <a href="/wiki/Amun" title="Amun">Amun</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Oracle" title="Oracle">Oracle</a> of <a href="/wiki/Siwa_Oasis" title="Siwa Oasis">Siwa Oasis</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Libya" title="Ancient Libya">Libyan</a> desert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBosworth198871.E2.80.9374_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBosworth198871.E2.80.9374-65">[65]</a></sup> Henceforth, Alexander often referred to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zeus-Ammon" title="Zeus-Ammon">Zeus-Ammon</a> as his true father, and after his death, currency depicted him adorned with rams horn as a symbol of his divinity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> During his stay in Egypt, he founded <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria-by-Egypt</a>, which would become the prosperous capital of the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemaic Kingdom</a> after his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assyria_and_Babylonia">Assyria and Babylonia</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gaugamela" title="Battle of Gaugamela">Battle of Gaugamela</a></div>
<p>Leaving Egypt in 331 BC, Alexander marched eastward into <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> (now northern <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>) and again defeated Darius, at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gaugamela" title="Battle of Gaugamela">Battle of Gaugamela</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Darius once more fled the field, and Alexander chased him as far as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arbil" title="Arbil">Arbela</a>. Gaugamela would be the final and decisive encounter between the two. Darius fled over the mountains to <a href="/wiki/Ecbatana" title="Ecbatana">Ecbatana</a> (modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hamedan" title="Hamedan">Hamedan</a>), while Alexander captured <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIII16_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-AIII16-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Persia">Persia</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2persian_gate_wall.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="531" data-file-width="800" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/2persian_gate_wall.JPG/220px-2persian_gate_wall.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/2persian_gate_wall.JPG/330px-2persian_gate_wall.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/2persian_gate_wall.JPG/440px-2persian_gate_wall.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2persian_gate_wall.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Site of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_Gate" title="Persian Gate">Persian Gate</a>; the road was built in the 1990s.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Persian_Gate" title="Battle of the Persian Gate">Battle of the Persian Gate</a></div>
<p>From Babylon, Alexander went to <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid</a> capitals, and captured its treasury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIII16_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-AIII16-69">[69]</a></sup> He sent the bulk of his army to the Persian ceremonial capital of <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a> via the Persian <a href="/wiki/Royal_Road" title="Royal Road">Royal Road</a>. Alexander himself took selected troops on the direct route to the city. He then stormed the pass of the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gates" title="Persian Gates">Persian Gates</a> (in the modern <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains" title="Zagros Mountains">Zagros Mountains</a>) which had been blocked by a Persian army under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ariobarzanes_(satrap_of_Persis)" title="Ariobarzanes (satrap of Persis)">Ariobarzanes</a> and then hurried to Persepolis before its garrison could loot the treasury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>On entering Persepolis, Alexander allowed his troops to loot the city for several days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Alexander stayed in Persepolis for five months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996121_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996121-72">[72]</a></sup> During his stay a fire broke out in the eastern palace of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xerxes_I_of_Persia" title="Xerxes I of Persia">Xerxes</a> and spread to the rest of the city. Possible causes include a drunken accident or deliberate revenge for the burning of the <a href="/wiki/Acropolis_of_Athens" title="Acropolis of Athens">Acropolis of Athens</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">Second Persian War</a> by <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammond198372.E2.80.9373_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammond198372.E2.80.9373-73">[73]</a></sup> Years later upon revisiting the city he had burnt, Alexander would regret the burning of Persepolis.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> recounts an anecdote in which Alexander pauses and talks to a fallen statue of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xerxes_the_Great" title="Xerxes the Great">Xerxes the Great</a> as if it were a live person:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Shall I pass by and leave you lying there because of the expeditions you led against Greece, or shall I set you up again because of your magnanimity and your virtues in other respects?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fall_of_the_Empire_and_the_East">Fall of the Empire and the East</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AlexanderCoin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="775" data-file-width="1489" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/AlexanderCoin.jpg/220px-AlexanderCoin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/AlexanderCoin.jpg/330px-AlexanderCoin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/AlexanderCoin.jpg/440px-AlexanderCoin.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AlexanderCoin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Silver_coin" title="Silver coin">Silver coin</a> of Alexander wearing the lion scalp of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herakles" title="Herakles">Herakles</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alexander then chased Darius, first into Media, and then Parthia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III.2C_19.E2.80.9320_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III.2C_19.E2.80.9320-75">[75]</a></sup> The Persian king no longer controlled his own destiny, and was taken prisoner by <a href="/wiki/Bessus" title="Bessus">Bessus</a>, his <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactrian</a> satrap and kinsman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III.2C_21_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III.2C_21-76">[76]</a></sup> As Alexander approached, Bessus had his men fatally stab the Great King and then declared himself Darius' successor as Artaxerxes V, before retreating into Central Asia to launch a <a href="/wiki/Guerrilla_warfare" title="Guerrilla warfare">guerrilla</a> campaign against Alexander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III.2C_21.2C_25_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III.2C_21.2C_25-77">[77]</a></sup> Alexander buried Darius' remains next to his Achaemenid predecessors in a regal funeral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III.2C_22_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III.2C_22-78">[78]</a></sup> He claimed that, while dying, Darius had named him as his successor to the Achaemenid throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGergel200481_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGergel200481-79">[79]</a></sup> The Achaemenid Empire is normally considered to have fallen with Darius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Alexander viewed Bessus as a usurper and set out to defeat him. This campaign, initially against Bessus, turned into a grand tour of central Asia. Alexander founded a series of new cities, all called Alexandria, including modern <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar</a> in Afghanistan, and <a href="/wiki/Alexandria_Eschate" title="Alexandria Eschate">Alexandria Eschate</a> ("The Furthest") in modern <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>. The campaign took Alexander through <a href="/wiki/Media_(region)" title="Media (region)">Media</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aria_(satrapy)" title="Aria (satrapy)">Aria</a> (West Afghanistan), <a href="/wiki/Drangiana" title="Drangiana">Drangiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arachosia" title="Arachosia">Arachosia</a> (South and Central Afghanistan), <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a> (North and Central Afghanistan), and <a href="/wiki/Scythia" title="Scythia">Scythia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III.2C_23.E2.80.9325.2C_27.E2.80.9330.3B_IV.2C_1.E2.80.937_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III.2C_23.E2.80.9325.2C_27.E2.80.9330.3B_IV.2C_1.E2.80.937-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Spitamenes" title="Spitamenes">Spitamenes</a>, who held an undefined position in the satrapy of Sogdiana, in 329 BC betrayed Bessus to <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_I_Soter" title="Ptolemy I Soter">Ptolemy</a>, one of Alexander's trusted companions, and Bessus was executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III.2C_30_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III.2C_30-82">[82]</a></sup> However, when, at some point later, Alexander was on the <a href="/wiki/Syr_Darya" title="Syr Darya">Jaxartes</a> dealing with an incursion by a horse nomad army, Spitamenes raised Sogdiana in revolt. Alexander personally defeated the Scythians at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jaxartes" title="Battle of Jaxartes">Battle of Jaxartes</a> and immediately launched a campaign against Spitamenes, defeating him in the Battle of Gabai. After the defeat, Spitamenes was killed by his own men, who then sued for peace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArrian1976IV.2C_5.E2.80.936.2C_16.E2.80.9317_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArrian1976IV.2C_5.E2.80.936.2C_16.E2.80.9317-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Problems_and_plots">Problems and plots</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_killing_of_Cleitus_by_Andre_Castaigne_(1898-1899)_reduced.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1193" data-file-width="766" height="343" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/The_killing_of_Cleitus_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29_reduced.jpg/220px-The_killing_of_Cleitus_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29_reduced.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/The_killing_of_Cleitus_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29_reduced.jpg/330px-The_killing_of_Cleitus_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29_reduced.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/The_killing_of_Cleitus_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29_reduced.jpg/440px-The_killing_of_Cleitus_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29_reduced.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_killing_of_Cleitus_by_Andre_Castaigne_(1898-1899)_reduced.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The killing of <a href="/wiki/Cleitus_the_Black" title="Cleitus the Black">Cleitus</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Castaigne" title="André Castaigne">André Castaigne</a> 1898–1899</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During this time, Alexander adopted some elements of Persian dress and customs at his court, notably the custom of <i><a href="/wiki/Proskynesis" title="Proskynesis">proskynesis</a></i>, either a symbolic kissing of the hand, or prostration on the ground, that Persians showed to their social superiors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII11_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-AVII11-84">[84]</a></sup> The Greeks regarded the gesture as the province of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deities" title="Deities">deities</a> and believed that Alexander meant to deify himself by requiring it. This cost him the sympathies of many of his countrymen, and he eventually abandoned it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>A plot against his life was revealed, and one of his officers, <a href="/wiki/Philotas" title="Philotas">Philotas</a>, was executed for failing to alert Alexander. The death of the son necessitated the death of the father, and thus <a href="/wiki/Parmenion" title="Parmenion">Parmenion</a>, who had been charged with guarding the treasury at <a href="/wiki/Ecbatana" title="Ecbatana">Ecbatana</a>, was assassinated at Alexander's command, to prevent attempts at vengeance. Most infamously, Alexander personally killed the man who had saved his life at Granicus, <a href="/wiki/Cleitus_the_Black" title="Cleitus the Black">Cleitus the Black</a>, during a violent drunken altercation at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maracanda" title="Maracanda">Maracanda</a> (modern day <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a> in <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>), in which Cleitus accused Alexander of several judgemental mistakes and most especially, of having forgotten the Macedonian ways in favour of a corrupt oriental lifestyle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGergel200499_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGergel200499-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Later, in the Central Asian campaign, a second plot against his life was revealed, this one instigated by his own royal <a href="/wiki/Page_(servant)" title="Page (servant)">pages</a>. His official historian, <a href="/wiki/Callisthenes" title="Callisthenes">Callisthenes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Olynthus" title="Olynthus">Olynthus</a>, was implicated in the plot; however, historians have yet to reach a consensus regarding this involvement. Callisthenes had fallen out of favour by leading the opposition to the attempt to introduce proskynesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Macedon_in_Alexander.27s_absence">Macedon in Alexander's absence</span></h3>
<p>When Alexander set out for Asia, he left his general <a href="/wiki/Antipater" title="Antipater">Antipater</a>, an experienced military and political leader and part of Philip II's "Old Guard", in charge of Macedon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_199_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_199-51">[51]</a></sup> Alexander's sacking of Thebes ensured that Greece remained quiet during his absence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_199_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_199-51">[51]</a></sup> The one exception was a call to arms by Spartan king <a href="/wiki/Agis_III" title="Agis III">Agis III</a> in 331 BC, whom Antipater defeated and killed in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Megalopolis" title="Battle of Megalopolis">battle of Megalopolis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_199_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_199-51">[51]</a></sup> Antipater referred the Spartans' punishment to the League of Corinth, which then deferred to Alexander, who chose to pardon them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> There was also considerable friction between Antipater and Olympias, and each complained to Alexander about the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>In general, Greece enjoyed a period of peace and prosperity during Alexander's campaign in Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Alexander sent back vast sums from his conquest, which stimulated the economy and increased trade across his empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> However, Alexander's constant demands for troops and the migration of Macedonians throughout his empire depleted Macedon's manpower, greatly weakening it in the years after Alexander, and ultimately led to its subjugation by Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_campaign">Indian campaign</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Indian_campaign_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Indian campaign of Alexander the Great">Indian campaign of Alexander the Great</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forays_into_the_Indian_subcontinent">Forays into the Indian subcontinent</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_phalanx_attacking_the_centre_in_the_battle_of_the_Hydaspes_by_Andre_Castaigne_(1898-1899).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2244" data-file-width="1534" height="322" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/The_phalanx_attacking_the_centre_in_the_battle_of_the_Hydaspes_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg/220px-The_phalanx_attacking_the_centre_in_the_battle_of_the_Hydaspes_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/The_phalanx_attacking_the_centre_in_the_battle_of_the_Hydaspes_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg/330px-The_phalanx_attacking_the_centre_in_the_battle_of_the_Hydaspes_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/The_phalanx_attacking_the_centre_in_the_battle_of_the_Hydaspes_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg/440px-The_phalanx_attacking_the_centre_in_the_battle_of_the_Hydaspes_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_phalanx_attacking_the_centre_in_the_battle_of_the_Hydaspes_by_Andre_Castaigne_(1898-1899).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The <a href="/wiki/Phalanx" title="Phalanx">phalanx</a> attacking the centre in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Hydaspes" title="Battle of the Hydaspes">Battle of the Hydaspes</a></i> by André Castaigne (1898–1899)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the death of <a href="/wiki/Spitamenes" title="Spitamenes">Spitamenes</a> and his marriage to Roxana (Raoxshna in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Iranian" title="Old Iranian">Old Iranian</a>) to cement relations with his new satrapies, Alexander turned to the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>. He invited the <a href="/wiki/Tribal_chief" title="Tribal chief">chieftains</a> of the former satrapy of <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a> (a region presently straddling eastern <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>), to come to him and submit to his authority. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omphis" title="Omphis">Omphis</a> (Indian name <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambhi" title="Ambhi">Ambhi</a>), the ruler of <a href="/wiki/Taxila" title="Taxila">Taxila</a>, whose kingdom extended from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus" title="Indus">Indus</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Jhelum_River" title="Jhelum River">Hydaspes (Jhelum)</a>, complied, but the chieftains of some hill clans, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aspasioi" title="Aspasioi">Aspasioi</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assakenoi" title="Assakenoi">Assakenoi</a> sections of the <a href="/wiki/Kambojas" title="Kambojas">Kambojas</a> (known in Indian texts also as Ashvayanas and Ashvakayanas), refused to submit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETripathi1999118.E2.80.9321_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETripathi1999118.E2.80.9321-92">[92]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambhi" title="Ambhi">Ambhi</a> hastened to relieve Alexander of his apprehension and met him with valuable presents, placing himself and all his forces at his disposal. Alexander not only returned Ambhi his title and the gifts but he also presented him with a wardrobe of "Persian robes, gold and silver ornaments, 30 horses and 1000 talents in gold". Alexander was emboldened to divide his forces, and Ambhi assisted <a href="/wiki/Hephaestion" title="Hephaestion">Hephaestion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perdiccas" title="Perdiccas">Perdiccas</a> in constructing a bridge over the Indus where it bends at Hund (Fox 1973), supplied their troops with provisions, and received Alexander himself, and his whole army, in his capital city of Taxila, with every demonstration of friendship and the most liberal hospitality.</p>
<p>On the subsequent advance of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedonian</a> king, Taxiles accompanied him with a force of 5000 men and took part in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Hydaspes_River" title="Battle of the Hydaspes River">battle of the Hydaspes River</a>. After that victory he was sent by Alexander in pursuit of Porus, to whom he was charged to offer favourable terms, but narrowly escaped losing his life at the hands of his old enemy. Subsequently, however, the two rivals were reconciled by the personal mediation of Alexander; and Taxiles, after having contributed zealously to the equipment of the fleet on the Hydaspes, was entrusted by the king with the government of the whole territory between that river and the Indus. A considerable accession of power was granted him after the death of <a href="/wiki/Philip_(son_of_Machatas)" title="Philip (son of Machatas)">Philip</a>, son of Machatas; and he was allowed to retain his authority at the death of Alexander himself (323 BC), as well as in the subsequent partition of the provinces at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Triparadisus" title="Treaty of Triparadisus">Triparadisus</a>, 321 BC.</p>
<p>In the winter of 327/326 BC, Alexander personally led a campaign against these clans; the Aspasioi of <a href="/wiki/Kunar_Valley" title="Kunar Valley">Kunar</a> <a href="/wiki/Valley" title="Valley">valleys</a>, the Guraeans of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panjkora" title="Panjkora">Guraeus</a> valley, and the Assakenoi of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swat_(Pakistan)" title="Swat (Pakistan)">Swat</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buner_Valley" title="Buner Valley">Buner</a> valleys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> A fierce contest ensued with the Aspasioi in which Alexander was wounded in the shoulder by a dart, but eventually the Aspasioi lost. Alexander then faced the Assakenoi, who fought in the strongholds of Massaga, Ora and <a href="/wiki/Aornos" title="Aornos">Aornos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETripathi1999118.E2.80.9321_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETripathi1999118.E2.80.9321-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>The fort of Massaga was reduced only after days of bloody fighting, in which Alexander was wounded seriously in the ankle. According to <a href="/wiki/Quintus_Curtius_Rufus" title="Quintus Curtius Rufus">Curtius</a>, "Not only did Alexander slaughter the entire population of Massaga, but also did he reduce its buildings to rubble."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> A similar slaughter followed at Ora. In the aftermath of Massaga and Ora, numerous Assakenians fled to the fortress of <a href="/wiki/Aornos" title="Aornos">Aornos</a>. Alexander followed close behind and captured the strategic hill-fort after four bloody days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETripathi1999118.E2.80.9321_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETripathi1999118.E2.80.9321-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AlexanderConquestsInIndia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1393" data-file-width="1117" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/AlexanderConquestsInIndia.jpg/220px-AlexanderConquestsInIndia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/AlexanderConquestsInIndia.jpg/330px-AlexanderConquestsInIndia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/AlexanderConquestsInIndia.jpg/440px-AlexanderConquestsInIndia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AlexanderConquestsInIndia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Alexander's invasion of the Indian subcontinent</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After Aornos, Alexander crossed the Indus and fought and won an epic battle against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Porus" title="King Porus">King Porus</a>, who ruled a region lying between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydaspes" title="Hydaspes">Hydaspes</a> and the Acesines (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chenab" title="Chenab">Chenab</a>), in what is now the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_region" title="Punjab region">Punjab</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Hydaspes" title="Battle of the Hydaspes">Battle of the Hydaspes</a> in 326 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETripathi1999124.E2.80.9325_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETripathi1999124.E2.80.9325-95">[95]</a></sup> Alexander was impressed by Porus' bravery, and made him an ally.
