He appointed Porus as satrap, and added to Porus' territory land that he did not previously own, towards the south-east, up to the Hyphasis (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beas" title="Beas">Beas</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-97">[97]</a></sup> Choosing a local helped him control these lands so distant from Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETripathi1999126.E2.80.9327_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETripathi1999126.E2.80.9327-98">[98]</a></sup> Alexander founded two cities on opposite sides of the <a href="/wiki/Jhelum_River" title="Jhelum River">Hydaspes</a> river, naming one <a href="/wiki/Alexandria_Bucephalous" title="Alexandria Bucephalous">Bucephala</a>, in honour of his horse, who died around this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGergel2004120_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGergel2004120-99">[99]</a></sup> The other was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicaea_(Punjab)" title="Nicaea (Punjab)">Nicaea</a> (Victory), thought to be located at the site of modern-day <a href="/wiki/Mong,_Punjab" title="Mong, Punjab">Mong, Punjab</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revolt_of_the_army">Revolt of the army</span></h3>
<p>East of Porus' kingdom, near the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ganges_River" title="Ganges River">Ganges River</a>, were the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanda_dynasty" title="Nanda dynasty">Nanda Empire</a> of <a href="/wiki/Magadha" title="Magadha">Magadha</a> and further east the <a href="/wiki/Gangaridai" title="Gangaridai">Gangaridai Empire</a> (of the modern-day <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a> region of the Indian subcontinent). Fearing the prospect of facing other large armies and exhausted by years of campaigning, Alexander's army mutinied at the <a href="/wiki/Beas_River" title="Beas River">Hyphasis River (Beas)</a>, refusing to march farther east. This river thus marks the easternmost extent of Alexander's conquests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETripathi1999129.E2.80.9330_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETripathi1999129.E2.80.9330-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>As for the Macedonians, however, their struggle with Porus blunted their courage and stayed their further advance into India. For having had all they could do to repulse an enemy who mustered only twenty thousand infantry and two thousand horse, they violently opposed Alexander when he insisted on crossing the river Ganges also, the width of which, as they learned, was thirty-two furlongs, its depth a hundred fathoms, while its banks on the further side were covered with multitudes of <a href="/wiki/Man-at-arms" title="Man-at-arms">men-at-arms</a> and horsemen and elephants. For they were told that the kings of the Ganderites and Praesii were awaiting them with eighty thousand horsemen, two hundred thousand footmen, eight thousand chariots, and six thousand <a href="/wiki/War_elephant" title="War elephant">war elephants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA62_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA62-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Alexander tried to persuade his soldiers to march farther, but his general <a href="/wiki/Coenus_(general)" title="Coenus (general)">Coenus</a> pleaded with him to change his opinion and return; the men, he said, "longed to again see their parents, their wives and children, their homeland". Alexander eventually agreed and turned south, marching along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_river" title="Indus river">Indus</a>. Along the way his army conquered the <a href="/wiki/Malhi" title="Malhi">Malhi</a> (in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Multan" title="Multan">Multan</a>) and other Indian tribes and Alexander sustained an injury during the siege.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETripathi1999137.E2.80.9338_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETripathi1999137.E2.80.9338-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Alexander sent much of his army to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kerman_province" title="Kerman province">Carmania</a> (modern southern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>) with general <a href="/wiki/Craterus" title="Craterus">Craterus</a>, and commissioned a fleet to explore the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> shore under his admiral <a href="/wiki/Nearchus" title="Nearchus">Nearchus</a>, while he led the rest back to Persia through the more difficult southern route along the <a href="/wiki/Gedrosia" title="Gedrosia">Gedrosian Desert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Makran" title="Makran">Makran</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETripathi1999141_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETripathi1999141-104">[104]</a></sup> Alexander reached Susa in 324 BC, but not before losing many men to the harsh desert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_years_in_Persia">Last years in Persia</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_and_Hephaestion.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="500" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Alexander_and_Hephaestion.jpg/220px-Alexander_and_Hephaestion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Alexander_and_Hephaestion.jpg/330px-Alexander_and_Hephaestion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Alexander_and_Hephaestion.jpg/440px-Alexander_and_Hephaestion.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_and_Hephaestion.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Alexander, left, and <a href="/wiki/Hephaestion" title="Hephaestion">Hephaestion</a>, right</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Discovering that many of his <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satraps</a> and military governors had misbehaved in his absence, Alexander executed several of them as examples on his way to <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVI27_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-AVI27-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII4_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-AVII4-107">[107]</a></sup> As a gesture of thanks, he paid off the debts of his soldiers, and announced that he would send over-aged and disabled veterans back to Macedon, led by Craterus. His troops misunderstood his intention and mutinied at the town of <a href="/wiki/Opis" title="Opis">Opis</a>. They refused to be sent away and criticized his adoption of Persian customs and dress and the introduction of Persian officers and soldiers into Macedonian units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Worthington_2003_307_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Worthington_2003_307-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Valenciennes,_Pierre-Henri_de_-_Alexander_at_the_Tomb_of_Cyrus_the_Great_-_1796.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="361" data-file-width="800" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Valenciennes%2C_Pierre-Henri_de_-_Alexander_at_the_Tomb_of_Cyrus_the_Great_-_1796.jpg/330px-Valenciennes%2C_Pierre-Henri_de_-_Alexander_at_the_Tomb_of_Cyrus_the_Great_-_1796.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Valenciennes%2C_Pierre-Henri_de_-_Alexander_at_the_Tomb_of_Cyrus_the_Great_-_1796.jpg/495px-Valenciennes%2C_Pierre-Henri_de_-_Alexander_at_the_Tomb_of_Cyrus_the_Great_-_1796.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Valenciennes%2C_Pierre-Henri_de_-_Alexander_at_the_Tomb_of_Cyrus_the_Great_-_1796.jpg/660px-Valenciennes%2C_Pierre-Henri_de_-_Alexander_at_the_Tomb_of_Cyrus_the_Great_-_1796.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Valenciennes,_Pierre-Henri_de_-_Alexander_at_the_Tomb_of_Cyrus_the_Great_-_1796.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Painting of Alexander the Great at the desecrated tomb of <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After three days, unable to persuade his men to back down, Alexander gave Persians command posts in the army and conferred Macedonian military titles upon Persian units. The Macedonians quickly begged forgiveness, which Alexander accepted, and held a great banquet for several thousand of his men at which he and they ate together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_194_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_194-109">[109]</a></sup> In an attempt to craft a lasting harmony between his Macedonian and Persian subjects, Alexander <a href="/wiki/Susa_weddings" title="Susa weddings">held a mass marriage</a> of his senior officers to Persian and other noblewomen at Susa, but few of those marriages seem to have lasted much beyond a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII4_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-AVII4-107">[107]</a></sup> Meanwhile, upon his return to Persia, Alexander learned that guards of the tomb of <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pasargadae" title="Pasargadae">Pasargadae</a> had desecrated it, and swiftly executed them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVI29_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-AVI29-110">[110]</a></sup> Alexander admired Cyrus, from an early age reading Xenophon's <i><a href="/wiki/Cyropaedia" title="Cyropaedia">Cyropaedia</a></i>, which described Cyrus's heroism in battle and governance as a king and legislator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ulrich_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ulrich-111">[111]</a></sup> During his visit to Pasargadae Alexander ordered his architect <a href="/wiki/Aristobulus_of_Cassandreia" title="Aristobulus of Cassandreia">Aristobulus</a> to decorate the interior of the sepulchral chamber of Cyrus' tomb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ulrich_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ulrich-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>Afterwards, Alexander travelled to Ecbatana to retrieve the bulk of the Persian treasure. There, his closest friend and possible lover, <a href="/wiki/Hephaestion" title="Hephaestion">Hephaestion</a>, died of illness or poisoning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII14_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-AVII14-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Hephaestion's death devastated Alexander, and he ordered the preparation of an expensive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Funeral_pyre" title="Funeral pyre">funeral pyre</a> in Babylon, as well as a decree for public mourning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII14_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-AVII14-112">[112]</a></sup> Back in Babylon, Alexander planned a series of new campaigns, beginning with an invasion of Arabia, but he would not have a chance to realize them, as he died shortly thereafter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII19_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-AVII19-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_succession">Death and succession</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Death of Alexander the Great">Death of Alexander the Great</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mid-nineteenth_century_reconstruction_of_Alexander%27s_catafalque_based_on_the_description_by_Diodorus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1166" data-file-width="2977" height="86" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Mid-nineteenth_century_reconstruction_of_Alexander%27s_catafalque_based_on_the_description_by_Diodorus.jpg/220px-Mid-nineteenth_century_reconstruction_of_Alexander%27s_catafalque_based_on_the_description_by_Diodorus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Mid-nineteenth_century_reconstruction_of_Alexander%27s_catafalque_based_on_the_description_by_Diodorus.jpg/330px-Mid-nineteenth_century_reconstruction_of_Alexander%27s_catafalque_based_on_the_description_by_Diodorus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Mid-nineteenth_century_reconstruction_of_Alexander%27s_catafalque_based_on_the_description_by_Diodorus.jpg/440px-Mid-nineteenth_century_reconstruction_of_Alexander%27s_catafalque_based_on_the_description_by_Diodorus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

19th century depiction of Alexander's funeral procession based on the description of Diodorus</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On either 10 or 11 June 323 BC, Alexander died in the palace of <a href="/wiki/Nebuchadnezzar_II" title="Nebuchadnezzar II">Nebuchadnezzar II</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>, at age 32.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Depuydt_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Depuydt-115">[115]</a></sup> There are two different versions of Alexander's death and details of the death differ slightly in each. <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>'s account is that roughly 14 days before his death, Alexander entertained admiral <a href="/wiki/Nearchus" title="Nearchus">Nearchus</a>, and spent the night and next day drinking with <a href="/wiki/Medius_of_Larissa" title="Medius of Larissa">Medius of Larissa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P75_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-P75-116">[116]</a></sup> He developed a fever, which worsened until he was unable to speak. The common soldiers, anxious about his health, were granted the right to file past him as he silently waved at them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood20012267.E2.80.9370_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood20012267.E2.80.9370-117">[117]</a></sup> In the second account, Diodorus recounts that Alexander was struck with pain after downing a large bowl of unmixed wine in honour of <a href="/wiki/Heracles" title="Heracles">Heracles</a>, followed by 11 days of weakness; he did not develop a fever and died after some agony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSXVII117_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVII117-118">[118]</a></sup> Arrian also mentioned this as an alternative, but Plutarch specifically denied this claim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P75_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-P75-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>Given the propensity of the Macedonian aristocracy to assassination,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen20071.E2.80.932_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen20071.E2.80.932-119">[119]</a></sup> foul play featured in multiple accounts of his death. Diodorus, Plutarch, Arrian and Justin all mentioned the theory that Alexander was poisoned. Justin stated that Alexander was the victim of a poisoning conspiracy, Plutarch dismissed it as a fabrication,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA77_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA77-120">[120]</a></sup> while both Diodorus and Arrian noted that they mentioned it only for the sake of completeness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSXVII117_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVII117-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII27_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-AVII27-121">[121]</a></sup> The accounts were nevertheless fairly consistent in designating Antipater, recently removed as Macedonian viceroy, and at odds with Olympias, as the head of the alleged plot. Perhaps taking his summons to Babylon as a death sentence,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200723.E2.80.9324_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200723.E2.80.9324-122">[122]</a></sup> and having seen the fate of Parmenion and Philotas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSXVII118_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVII118-123">[123]</a></sup> Antipater purportedly arranged for Alexander to be poisoned by his son Iollas, who was Alexander's wine-pourer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII27_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-AVII27-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSXVII118_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVII118-123">[123]</a></sup> There was even a suggestion that Aristotle may have participated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII27_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-AVII27-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>The strongest argument against the poison theory is the fact that twelve days passed between the start of his illness and his death; such long-acting poisons were probably not available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFox2006chapter_32_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFox2006chapter_32-124">[124]</a></sup> However, in a 2003 BBC documentary investigating the death of Alexander, Leo Schep from the New Zealand National Poisons Centre proposed that the plant white hellebore (<i><a href="/wiki/Veratrum_album" title="Veratrum album">Veratrum album</a></i>), which was known in antiquity, may have been used to poison Alexander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECawthorne2004138_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECawthorne2004138-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> In a 2014 manuscript in the journal <a href="/wiki/Clinical_Toxicology" title="Clinical Toxicology">Clinical Toxicology</a> Schep suggested Alexander's wine was spiked with <i>Veratrum album</i>, and that this would produce poisoning symptoms that match the course of events described in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_Romance" title="Alexander Romance">Alexander Romance</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schep_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-schep-128">[128]</a></sup> <i>Veratrum album</i> poisoning can have a prolonged course and it was suggested that if Alexander was poisoned, <i>Veratrum album</i> offers the most plausible cause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schep_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-schep-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Another poisoning explanation put forward in 2010 proposed that the circumstances of his death were compatible with poisoning by water of the river Styx (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Mavroneri" title="Mavroneri">Mavroneri</a> in Arcadia, Greece) that contained <a href="/wiki/Calicheamicin" title="Calicheamicin">calicheamicin</a>, a dangerous compound produced by bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_causes" title="Natural causes">natural causes</a> (diseases) have been suggested, including <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever" title="Typhoid fever">typhoid fever</a>. A 1998 article in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_England_Journal_of_Medicine" title="New England Journal of Medicine">New England Journal of Medicine</a></i> attributed his death to typhoid fever complicated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bowel_perforation" title="Bowel perforation">bowel perforation</a> and ascending <a href="/wiki/Paralysis" title="Paralysis">paralysis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMD_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMD-131">[131]</a></sup> Another recent analysis suggested pyogenic (infectious) <a href="/wiki/Spondylitis" title="Spondylitis">spondylitis</a> or <a href="/wiki/Meningitis" title="Meningitis">meningitis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ashrafian_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-ashrafian-132">[132]</a></sup> Other illnesses fit the symptoms, including <a href="/wiki/Acute_pancreatitis" title="Acute pancreatitis">acute pancreatitis</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Nile_virus" title="West Nile virus">West Nile virus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref1_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-ref1-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Natural-cause theories also tend to emphasize that Alexander's health may have been in general decline after years of heavy drinking and severe wounds. The anguish that Alexander felt after <a href="/wiki/Hephaestion" title="Hephaestion">Hephaestion</a>'s death may also have contributed to his declining health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMD_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-AMD-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_death">After death</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Tomb of Alexander the Great">Tomb of Alexander the Great</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Sarcophagus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1440" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Alexander_Sarcophagus.jpg/220px-Alexander_Sarcophagus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Alexander_Sarcophagus.jpg/330px-Alexander_Sarcophagus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Alexander_Sarcophagus.jpg/440px-Alexander_Sarcophagus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Sarcophagus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Detail of Alexander on the <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Sarcophagus" title="Alexander Sarcophagus">Alexander Sarcophagus</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alexander's body was laid in a gold anthropoid <a href="/wiki/Sarcophagus" title="Sarcophagus">sarcophagus</a> that was filled with honey, which was in turn placed in a gold casket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sarco1_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-sarco1-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> According to Aelian, a seer called Aristander foretold that the land where Alexander was laid to rest "would be happy and unvanquishable forever".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aelian_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aelian-137">[137]</a></sup> Perhaps more likely, the successors may have seen possession of the body as a symbol of legitimacy, since burying the prior king was a <a href="/wiki/Royal_prerogative" title="Royal prerogative">royal prerogative</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200732_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200732-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>While Alexander's funeral cortege was on its way to Macedon, Ptolemy seized it and took it temporarily to Memphis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sarco1_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-sarco1-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aelian_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aelian-137">[137]</a></sup> His successor, <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_II_Philadelphus" title="Ptolemy II Philadelphus">Ptolemy II Philadelphus</a>, transferred the sarcophagus to Alexandria, where it remained until at least <a href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">late Antiquity</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_IX_Lathyros" title="Ptolemy IX Lathyros">Ptolemy IX Lathyros</a>, one of Ptolemy's final successors, replaced Alexander's sarcophagus with a glass one so he could convert the original to coinage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sarco2_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-sarco2-139">[139]</a></sup> The recent discovery of an <a href="/wiki/Kasta_Tomb" title="Kasta Tomb">enormous tomb</a> in northern Greece, at <a href="/wiki/Amphipolis" title="Amphipolis">Amphipolis</a>, dating from the time of Alexander the Great <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> has given rise to speculation that its original intent was to be the burial place of Alexander. This would fit with the intended destination of Alexander's funeral cortege.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a> all visited the tomb in Alexandria, where Augustus, allegedly, accidentally knocked the nose off. <a href="/wiki/Caligula" title="Caligula">Caligula</a> was said to have taken Alexander's breastplate from the tomb for his own use. Around AD 200, Emperor <a href="/wiki/Septimius_Severus" title="Septimius Severus">Septimius Severus</a> closed Alexander's tomb to the public. His son and successor, <a href="/wiki/Caracalla" title="Caracalla">Caracalla</a>, a great admirer, visited the tomb during his own reign. After this, details on the fate of the tomb are hazy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sarco2_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-sarco2-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>The so-called "<a href="/wiki/Alexander_Sarcophagus" title="Alexander Sarcophagus">Alexander Sarcophagus</a>", discovered near <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">Sidon</a> and now in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Istanbul_Archaeology_Museum" title="Istanbul Archaeology Museum">Istanbul Archaeology Museum</a>, is so named not because it was thought to have contained Alexander's remains, but because its bas-reliefs depict Alexander and his companions fighting the Persians and hunting. It was originally thought to have been the sarcophagus of <a href="/wiki/Abdalonymus" title="Abdalonymus">Abdalonymus</a> (died 311 BC), the king of Sidon appointed by Alexander immediately following the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Issus" title="Battle of Issus">battle of Issus</a> in 331.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> However, more recently, it has been suggested that it may date from earlier than Abdalonymus' death.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Division_of_the_empire">Division of the empire</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Babylon" title="Partition of Babylon">Partition of Babylon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diadochi" title="Diadochi">Diadochi</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diadochen1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="961" data-file-width="2000" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Diadochen1.png/220px-Diadochen1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Diadochen1.png/330px-Diadochen1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Diadochen1.png/440px-Diadochen1.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diadochen1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Kingdoms of the <a href="/wiki/Diadochi" title="Diadochi">Diadochi</a> in 281 BC: the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemaic Kingdom</a> (dark blue), the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a> (yellow), <a href="/wiki/Pergamon" title="Pergamon">Kingdom of Pergamon</a> (orange), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Macedon" title="Kingdom of Macedon">Kingdom of Macedon</a> (green). Also shown are the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> (light blue), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carthaginian_Empire" title="Carthaginian Empire">Carthaginian Republic</a> (purple), and the <a href="/wiki/Epirus_(ancient_state)" title="Epirus (ancient state)">Kingdom of Epirus</a> (red).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alexander's death was so sudden that when reports of his death reached Greece, they were not immediately believed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_199_51-4"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_199-51">[51]</a></sup> Alexander had no obvious or legitimate heir, his son Alexander IV by Roxane being born after Alexander's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200724.E2.80.9326_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200724.E2.80.9326-143">[143]</a></sup> According to Diodorus, Alexander's companions asked him on his deathbed to whom he bequeathed his kingdom; his laconic reply was "tôi kratistôi"—"to the strongest".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSXVII117_118-2"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVII117-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>Arrian and Plutarch claimed that Alexander was speechless by this point, implying that this was an apocryphal story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> Diodorus, Curtius and Justin offered the more plausible story that Alexander passed his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seal_(device)" title="Seal (device)">signet ring</a> to <a href="/wiki/Perdiccas" title="Perdiccas">Perdiccas</a>, a bodyguard and leader of the companion cavalry, in front of witnesses, thereby nominating him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSXVII117_118-3"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVII117-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200724.E2.80.9326_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200724.E2.80.9326-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>Perdiccas initially did not claim power, instead suggesting that Roxane's baby would be king, if male; with himself, <a href="/wiki/Craterus" title="Craterus">Craterus</a>, Leonnatus, and Antipater as guardians. However, the infantry, under the command of <a href="/wiki/Meleager_(general)" title="Meleager (general)">Meleager</a>, rejected this arrangement since they had been excluded from the discussion. Instead, they supported Alexander's half-brother Philip Arrhidaeus. Eventually, the two sides reconciled, and after the birth of Alexander IV, he and Philip III were appointed joint kings, albeit in name only.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200726.E2.80.9329_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200726.E2.80.9329-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>Dissension and rivalry soon afflicted the Macedonians, however. The satrapies handed out by Perdiccas at the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Babylon" title="Partition of Babylon">Partition of Babylon</a> became power bases each general used to bid for power. After the assassination of Perdiccas in 321 BC, Macedonian unity collapsed, and 40 years of war between "The Successors" (<i>Diadochi</i>) ensued before the Hellenistic world settled into four stable power blocks: <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemaic</a> Egypt, <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid</a> Mesopotamia and Central Asia, <a href="/wiki/Attalid_dynasty" title="Attalid dynasty">Attalid</a> Anatolia, and <a href="/wiki/Antigonid_dynasty" title="Antigonid dynasty">Antigonid</a> Macedon. In the process, both Alexander IV and Philip III were murdered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200729.E2.80.9334_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200729.E2.80.9334-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Testament">Testament</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Agathokles_commemorative_coin_for_Alexander_the_Great.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="800" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Agathokles_commemorative_coin_for_Alexander_the_Great.jpg/220px-Agathokles_commemorative_coin_for_Alexander_the_Great.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Agathokles_commemorative_coin_for_Alexander_the_Great.jpg/330px-Agathokles_commemorative_coin_for_Alexander_the_Great.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Agathokles_commemorative_coin_for_Alexander_the_Great.jpg/440px-Agathokles_commemorative_coin_for_Alexander_the_Great.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Agathokles_commemorative_coin_for_Alexander_the_Great.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Commemorative coin by <a href="/wiki/Agathocles_of_Bactria" title="Agathocles of Bactria">Agathocles of Bactria</a> (190-180 BCE) for Alexander the Great.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus</a> stated that Alexander had given detailed written instructions to Craterus some time before his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSXVIII4_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVIII4-147">[147]</a></sup> Craterus started to carry out Alexander's commands, but the successors chose not to further implement them, on the grounds they were impractical and extravagant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSXVIII4_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVIII4-147">[147]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Perdiccas read Alexander's will to his troops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_199_51-5"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_199-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>The testament called for military expansion into the southern and western Mediterranean, monumental constructions, and the intermixing of Eastern and Western populations. It included:</p>
<ul>
<li>Construction of a monumental tomb for his father Philip, "to match the greatest of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_pyramids" title="Egyptian pyramids">pyramids of Egypt</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_199_51-6"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_199-51">[51]</a></sup></li>
<li>Erection of great temples in <a href="/wiki/Delos" title="Delos">Delos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delphi" title="Delphi">Delphi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dodona" title="Dodona">Dodona</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dium" title="Dium">Dium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amphipolis" title="Amphipolis">Amphipolis</a>, and a monumental temple to <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a> at <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Troy</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_199_51-7"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_199-51">[51]</a></sup></li>
<li>Conquest of Arabia and the entire Mediterranean Basin<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_199_51-8"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_199-51">[51]</a></sup></li>
<li>Circumnavigation of Africa<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_199_51-9"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_199-51">[51]</a></sup></li>
<li>Development of cities and the "transplant of populations from Asia to Europe and in the opposite direction from Europe to Asia, in order to bring the largest continent to common unity and to friendship by means of intermarriage and family ties."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Character">Character</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Generalship">Generalship</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Battle_granicus.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="573" data-file-width="746" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Battle_granicus.png/220px-Battle_granicus.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Battle_granicus.png/330px-Battle_granicus.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Battle_granicus.png/440px-Battle_granicus.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battle_granicus.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Granicus" title="Battle of the Granicus">Battle of the Granicus</a>, 334 BC</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Battle_issus_decisive.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="563" data-file-width="738" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Battle_issus_decisive.png/220px-Battle_issus_decisive.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Battle_issus_decisive.png/330px-Battle_issus_decisive.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Battle_issus_decisive.png/440px-Battle_issus_decisive.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battle_issus_decisive.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Issus" title="Battle of Issus">Battle of Issus</a>, 333 BC</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alexander earned the epithet "the Great" due to his unparalleled success as a military commander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192_50-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192-50">[50]</a></sup> He never lost a battle, despite typically being outnumbered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192_50-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192-50">[50]</a></sup> This was due to use of terrain, <a href="/wiki/Phalanx" title="Phalanx">phalanx</a> and cavalry tactics, bold strategy, and the fierce loyalty of his troops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_193_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_193-149">[149]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_phalanx" title="Macedonian phalanx">Macedonian phalanx</a>, armed with the <a href="/wiki/Sarissa" title="Sarissa">sarissa</a>, a spear 6 metres (20 ft) long, had been developed and perfected by Philip II through rigorous training,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996110_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996110-150">[150]</a></sup> and Alexander used its speed and maneuverability to great effect against larger but more disparate<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (April 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Persian forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996110_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996110-150">[150]</a></sup> Alexander also recognized the potential for disunity among his diverse army, which employed various languages and weapons. He overcame this by being personally involved in battle,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996121_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996121-72">[72]</a></sup> in the manner of a Macedonian king.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_193_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_193-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his first battle in Asia, at Granicus, Alexander used only a small part of his forces<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Plutarch and Polybios, citing earlier writers, give the strength as ranging from 30k foot and 4k horse to 43k foot and 5,500 horse (March 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, perhaps 13,000 infantry with 5,000 cavalry, against a much larger Persian force of 40,000. Alexander placed the phalanx at the center and cavalry and archers on the wings, so that his line matched the length of the Persian cavalry line, about 3 km (1.86 mi). By contrast, the Persian infantry was stationed behind its cavalry. This ensured that Alexander would not be outflanked, while his phalanx, armed with long pikes, had a considerable advantage over the Persian's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scimitars" title="Scimitars">scimitars</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Javelins" title="Javelins">javelins</a>. Macedonian losses were negligible compared to those of the Persians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996122_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996122-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p>At Issus in 333 BC, his first confrontation with Darius, he used the same deployment, and again the central phalanx pushed through.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996122_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996122-151">[151]</a></sup> Alexander personally led the charge in the center, routing the opposing army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010193_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010193-152">[152]</a></sup> At the decisive encounter with Darius at Gaugamela, Darius equipped his chariots with scythes on the wheels to break up the phalanx and equipped his cavalry with pikes. Alexander arranged a double phalanx, with the center advancing at an angle, parting when the chariots bore down and then reforming. The advance was successful and broke Darius' center, causing the latter to flee once again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996122_151-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996122-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p>When faced with opponents who used unfamiliar fighting techniques, such as in Central Asia and India, Alexander adapted his forces to his opponents' style. Thus, in <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdiana" title="Sogdiana">Sogdiana</a>, Alexander successfully used his javelin throwers and archers to prevent outflanking movements, while massing his cavalry at the center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010193_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010193-152">[152]</a></sup> In India, confronted by Porus' elephant corps, the Macedonians opened their ranks to envelop the elephants and used their sarissas to strike upwards and dislodge the elephants' handlers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_194_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_194-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_appearance">Physical appearance</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AlexandreLouvre.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="576" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/AlexandreLouvre.jpg/170px-AlexandreLouvre.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/AlexandreLouvre.jpg/255px-AlexandreLouvre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/AlexandreLouvre.jpg/340px-AlexandreLouvre.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AlexandreLouvre.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Roman copy of a <a href="/wiki/Herma" title="Herma">herma</a> by <a href="/wiki/Lysippos" title="Lysippos">Lysippos</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louvre_Museum" title="Louvre Museum">Louvre Museum</a>. Plutarch reports that sculptures by Lysippos were the most faithful.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Greek biographer <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> (c. 45–120 AD) describes Alexander's appearance as:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>¹ The outward appearance of Alexander is best represented by the statues of him which Lysippus made, and it was by this artist alone that Alexander himself thought it fit that he should be modelled. ² For those peculiarities which many of his successors and friends afterwards tried to imitate, namely, the poise of the neck, which was bent slightly to the left, and the melting glance of his eyes, this artist has accurately observed. ³ Apelles, however, in painting him as wielder of the thunder-bolt, did not reproduce his complexion, but made it too dark and swarthy. Whereas he was of a fair colour, as they say, and his fairness passed into ruddiness on his breast particularly, and in his face. <sup>4</sup> Moreover, that a very pleasant odour exhaled from his skin and that there was a fragrance about his mouth and all his flesh, so that his garments were filled with it, this we have read in the <i>Memoirs of Aristoxenus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.perseus.tufts.edu.2Fhopper.2Ftext.3Fdoc.3DPerseus:text:1999.01.0243:chapter.3D4.26highlight.3Dlysippus_IV.2C_1.5D_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.perseus.tufts.edu.2Fhopper.2Ftext.3Fdoc.3DPerseus:text:1999.01.0243:chapter.3D4.26highlight.3Dlysippus_IV.2C_1.5D-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Arrian" title="Arrian">Arrian</a> (Lucius Flavius Arrianus 'Xenophon' c. 86–160) described Alexander as:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>[T]he strong, handsome commander with one eye dark as the night and one blue as the sky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The semi-legendary <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_Romance" title="Alexander Romance">Alexander Romance</a></i> also suggests that Alexander suffered from <a href="/wiki/Heterochromia_iridum" title="Heterochromia iridum">heterochromia iridum</a>: that one eye was dark and the other light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrafton201027_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrafton201027-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>British historian <a href="/wiki/Peter_Green_(historian)" title="Peter Green (historian)">Peter Green</a> provided a description of Alexander's appearance, based on his review of statues and some ancient documents:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Physically, Alexander was not prepossessing. Even by Macedonian standards he was very short, though stocky and tough. His beard was scanty, and he stood out against his hirsute Macedonian barons by going clean-shaven. His neck was in some way twisted, so that he appeared to be gazing upward at an angle. His eyes (one blue, one brown) revealed a dewy, feminine quality. He had a high complexion and a harsh voice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200715.E2.80.9316_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200715.E2.80.9316-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Ancient authors recorded that Alexander was so pleased with portraits of himself created by <a href="/wiki/Lysippos" title="Lysippos">Lysippos</a> that he forbade other sculptors from crafting his image.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> Lysippos had often used the <a href="/wiki/Contrapposto" title="Contrapposto">contrapposto</a> sculptural scheme to portray Alexander and other characters such as <a href="/wiki/Apoxyomenos" title="Apoxyomenos">Apoxyomenos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hermes" title="Hermes">Hermes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eros" title="Eros">Eros</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> Lysippos' sculpture, famous for its naturalism, as opposed to a stiffer, more static pose, is thought to be the most faithful depiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBosworth198819.E2.80.9320_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBosworth198819.E2.80.9320-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personality">Personality</span></h3>
<p>Some of Alexander's strongest personality traits formed in response to his parents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200715.E2.80.9316_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200715.E2.80.9316-157">[157]</a></sup> His mother had huge ambitions, and encouraged him to believe it was his destiny to conquer the Persian Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200715.E2.80.9316_157-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200715.E2.80.9316-157">[157]</a></sup> Olympias' influence instilled a sense of destiny in him,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen20074_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen20074-161">[161]</a></sup> and Plutarch tells us that his ambition "kept his spirit serious and lofty in advance of his years".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA4_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA4-162">[162]</a></sup> However, his father Philip was Alexander's most immediate and influential role model, as the young Alexander watched him campaign practically every year, winning victory after victory while ignoring severe wounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010190_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010190-38">[38]</a></sup> Alexander's relationship with his father forged the competitive side of his personality; he had a need to out-do his father, illustrated by his reckless behaviour in battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200715.E2.80.9316_157-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200715.E2.80.9316-157">[157]</a></sup> While Alexander worried that his father would leave him "no great or brilliant achievement to be displayed to the world",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA5_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA5-163">[163]</a></sup> he also downplayed his father's achievements to his companions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200715.E2.80.9316_157-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200715.E2.80.9316-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lion_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="384" data-file-width="850" height="99" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Lion_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg/220px-Lion_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Lion_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg/330px-Lion_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Lion_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg/440px-Lion_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lion_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Alexander (left), wearing a <i><a href="/wiki/Kausia" title="Kausia">kausia</a></i> and fighting an <a href="/wiki/Asiatic_lion" title="Asiatic lion">Asiatic lion</a> with his friend <a href="/wiki/Craterus" title="Craterus">Craterus</a> (detail); late 4th century BC <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pella" title="Pella">Pella</a> Museum.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to Plutarch, among Alexander's traits were a violent temper and rash, impulsive nature,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII29_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-AVII29-165">[165]</a></sup> which undoubtedly contributed to some of his decisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200715.E2.80.9316_157-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200715.E2.80.9316-157">[157]</a></sup> Although Alexander was stubborn and did not respond well to orders from his father, he was open to reasoned debate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA7_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA7-166">[166]</a></sup> He had a calmer side—perceptive, logical, and calculating. He had a great desire for knowledge, a love for philosophy, and was an avid reader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA8_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA8-167">[167]</a></sup> This was no doubt in part due to Aristotle's tutelage; Alexander was intelligent and quick to learn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200715.E2.80.9316_157-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200715.E2.80.9316-157">[157]</a></sup> His intelligent and rational side was amply demonstrated by his ability and success as a general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII29_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-AVII29-165">[165]</a></sup> He had great self-restraint in "pleasures of the body", in contrast with his lack of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self_control" title="Self control">self control</a> with alcohol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII28_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-AVII28-168">[168]</a></sup></p>
<p>Alexander was erudite and patronized both arts and sciences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA4_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-PA4-162">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA8_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-PA8-167">[167]</a></sup> However, he had little interest in sports or the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Olympic_Games" title="Ancient Olympic Games">Olympic games</a> (unlike his father), seeking only the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeric" title="Homeric">Homeric</a> ideals of honour (<i>timê</i>) and glory (<i>kudos</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> He had great <a href="/wiki/Charisma" title="Charisma">charisma</a> and force of personality, characteristics which made him a great leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200724.E2.80.9326_143-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200724.E2.80.9326-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII29_165-2"><a href="#cite_note-AVII29-165">[165]</a></sup> His unique abilities were further demonstrated by the inability of any of his generals to unite Macedonia and retain the Empire after his death – only Alexander had the ability to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200724.E2.80.9326_143-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200724.E2.80.9326-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>During his final years, and especially after the death of Hephaestion, Alexander began to exhibit signs of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megalomania" title="Megalomania">megalomania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paranoia" title="Paranoia">paranoia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200723.E2.80.9324_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200723.E2.80.9324-122">[122]</a></sup> His extraordinary achievements, coupled with his own ineffable sense of destiny and the flattery of his companions, may have combined to produce this effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200720.E2.80.9321_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200720.E2.80.9321-170">[170]</a></sup> His <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Delusions_of_grandeur" title="Delusions of grandeur">delusions of grandeur</a> are readily visible in his <a href="#Testament">testament</a> and in his desire to conquer the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200723.E2.80.9324_122-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200723.E2.80.9324-122">[122]</a></sup> in as much as he is by various sources described as having <i>boundless ambition</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> an epithet, the meaning of which, has descended into an historical cliché.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup></p>
<p>He appears to have believed himself a deity, or at least sought to deify himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200723.E2.80.9324_122-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200723.E2.80.9324-122">[122]</a></sup> Olympias always insisted to him that he was the son of Zeus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA3_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA3-181">[181]</a></sup> a theory apparently confirmed to him by the oracle of Amun at <a href="/wiki/Siwa_Oasis" title="Siwa Oasis">Siwa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P27_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-P27-182">[182]</a></sup> He began to identify himself as the son of Zeus-Ammon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P27_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-P27-182">[182]</a></sup> Alexander adopted elements of Persian dress and customs at court, notably <i><a href="/wiki/Proskynesis" title="Proskynesis">proskynesis</a></i>, a practice of which Macedonians disapproved, and were loath to perform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII11_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-AVII11-84">[84]</a></sup> This behaviour cost him the sympathies of many of his countrymen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA45_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA45-183">[183]</a></sup> However, Alexander also was a pragmatic ruler who understood the difficulties of ruling culturally disparate peoples, many of whom lived in kingdoms where the king was divine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> Thus, rather than megalomania, his behaviour may simply have been a practical attempt at strengthening his rule and keeping his empire together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mosaica.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="333" data-file-width="268" height="273" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Mosaica.jpg/220px-Mosaica.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/75/Mosaica.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mosaica.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A mural in Pompeii, depicting the marriage of Alexander to Barsine (Stateira) in 324 BC. The couple are apparently dressed as Ares and Aphrodite.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_relationships">Personal relationships</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Personal_relationships_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Personal relationships of Alexander the Great">Personal relationships of Alexander the Great</a></div>
<p>Alexander married three times: <a href="/wiki/Roxana" title="Roxana">Roxana</a>, daughter of the <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdian</a> nobleman <a href="/wiki/Oxyartes" title="Oxyartes">Oxyartes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> out of love;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.perseus.tufts.edu.2Fhopper.2Ftext.3Fdoc.3DPerseus.253Atext.253A1999.01.0243.253Achapter.253D47_LXVII.2C_1.5D_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.perseus.tufts.edu.2Fhopper.2Ftext.3Fdoc.3DPerseus.253Atext.253A1999.01.0243.253Achapter.253D47_LXVII.2C_1.5D-189">[189]</a></sup> and the Persian princesses <a href="/wiki/Stateira_II" title="Stateira II">Stateira II</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parysatis_II" title="Parysatis II">Parysatis II</a>, the former a daughter of <a href="/wiki/Darius_III" title="Darius III">Darius III</a> and latter a daughter of <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_III" title="Artaxerxes III">Artaxerxes III</a>, for political reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carney_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carney-190">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1936.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fpenelope.uchicago.edu.2FThayer.2FE.2FRoman.2FTexts.2FPlutarch.2FMoralia.2FFortuna_Alexandri.2A.2F2.html_II.2C_6.5D_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1936.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fpenelope.uchicago.edu.2FThayer.2FE.2FRoman.2FTexts.2FPlutarch.2FMoralia.2FFortuna_Alexandri.2A.2F2.html_II.2C_6.5D-191">[191]</a></sup> He apparently had two sons, Alexander IV of Macedon of Roxana and, possibly, <a href="/wiki/Heracles_of_Macedon" title="Heracles of Macedon">Heracles of Macedon</a> from his mistress Barsine. He lost another child when Roxana miscarried at Babylon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERenault2001100_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERenault2001100-193">[193]</a></sup></p>
<p>Alexander also had a close relationship with his friend, general, and bodyguard <a href="/wiki/Hephaestion" title="Hephaestion">Hephaestion</a>, the son of a Macedonian noble.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII14_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-AVII14-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200715.E2.80.9316_157-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200715.E2.80.9316-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSXVII114_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVII114-194">[194]</a></sup> Hephaestion's death devastated Alexander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII14_112-3"><a href="#cite_note-AVII14-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P72_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-P72-195">[195]</a></sup> This event may have contributed to Alexander's failing health and detached <a href="/wiki/Mental_health" title="Mental health">mental state</a> during his final months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200723.E2.80.9324_122-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200723.E2.80.9324-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMD_131-2"><a href="#cite_note-AMD-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>Alexander's sexuality has been the subject of speculation and controversy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOgden2009204_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOgden2009204-196">[196]</a></sup> No ancient sources stated that Alexander had homosexual relationships, or that Alexander's relationship with Hephaestion was sexual. Aelian, however, writes of Alexander's visit to <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Troy</a> where "Alexander garlanded the tomb of Achilles and Hephaestion that of <a href="/wiki/Patroclus" title="Patroclus">Patroclus</a>, the latter riddling that he was a beloved of Alexander, in just the same way as Patroclus was of Achilles."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AelXII7_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-AelXII7-197">[197]</a></sup> Noting that the word <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eromenos" title="Eromenos">eromenos</a></i> (ancient Greek for beloved) does not necessarily bear sexual meaning, Alexander may have been <a href="/wiki/Bisexuality" title="Bisexuality">bisexual</a>, which in his time was not controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESacks199516_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESacks199516-198">[198]</a></sup></p>
<p>Green argues that there is little evidence in ancient sources that Alexander had much carnal interest in women; he did not produce an heir until the very end of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200715.E2.80.9316_157-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200715.E2.80.9316-157">[157]</a></sup> However, he was relatively young when he died, and Ogden suggests that Alexander's matrimonial record is more impressive than his father's at the same age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOgden2009208_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOgden2009208-199">[199]</a></sup> Apart from wives, Alexander had many more female companions. Alexander accumulated a harem in the style of Persian kings, but he used it rather sparingly,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSXVII77_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVII77-200">[200]</a></sup> showing great self-control in "pleasures of the body".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII28_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-AVII28-168">[168]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Plutarch described how Alexander was infatuated by Roxana while complimenting him on not forcing himself on her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1936.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fpenelope.uchicago.edu.2FThayer.2FE.2FRoman.2FTexts.2FPlutarch.2FMoralia.2FFortuna_Alexandri.2A.2F2.html_I.2C_11.5D_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1936.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fpenelope.uchicago.edu.2FThayer.2FE.2FRoman.2FTexts.2FPlutarch.2FMoralia.2FFortuna_Alexandri.2A.2F2.html_I.2C_11.5D-201">[201]</a></sup> Green suggested that, in the context of the period, Alexander formed quite strong friendships with women, including <a href="/wiki/Ada_of_Caria" title="Ada of Caria">Ada of Caria</a>, who adopted him, and even Darius' mother <a href="/wiki/Sisygambis" title="Sisygambis">Sisygambis</a>, who supposedly died from grief upon hearing of Alexander's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200715.E2.80.9316_157-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200715.E2.80.9316-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
