<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="611" data-file-width="1040" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg/220px-Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg/330px-Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg/440px-Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Hellenistic world view after Alexander: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_world_map" title="Ancient world map">ancient world map</a> of <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a> (276–194 BC), incorporating information from the campaigns of Alexander and his successors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alexander's legacy extended beyond his military conquests. His campaigns greatly increased contacts and trade between East and West, and vast areas to the east were significantly exposed to Greek civilization and influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186-16">[16]</a></sup> Some of the cities he founded became major cultural centers, many surviving into the 21st century. His chroniclers recorded valuable information about the areas through which he marched, while the Greeks themselves got a sense of belonging to a world beyond the Mediterranean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenistic_kingdoms">Hellenistic kingdoms</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a></div>
<p>Alexander's most immediate legacy was the introduction of Macedonian rule to huge new swathes of Asia. At the time of his death, Alexander's empire covered some 5,200,000 km<sup>2</sup> (2,000,000 sq mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uconn_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-uconn-203">[203]</a></sup> and was the largest state of its time. Many of these areas remained in Macedonian hands or under Greek influence for the next 200–300 years. The <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period#The_successors" title="Hellenistic period">successor states</a> that emerged were, at least initially, dominant forces, and these 300 years are often referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen2007xii.E2.80.93xix_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen2007xii.E2.80.93xix-204">[204]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="547" data-file-width="682" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg/220px-Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg/330px-Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg/440px-Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Plan of Alexandria c. 30 BC</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The eastern borders of Alexander's empire began to collapse even during his lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200724.E2.80.9326_143-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200724.E2.80.9326-143">[143]</a></sup> However, the power vacuum he left in the northwest of the Indian subcontinent directly gave rise to one of the most powerful Indian dynasties in history, the <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Maurya Empire</a>. Taking advantage of this power vacuum, <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_Maurya" title="Chandragupta Maurya">Chandragupta Maurya</a> (referred to in Greek sources as "Sandrokottos"), of relatively humble origin, took control of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_region" title="Punjab region">Punjab</a>, and with that power base proceeded to conquer the <a href="/wiki/Nanda_Empire" title="Nanda Empire">Nanda Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeay200182.E2.80.9385_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeay200182.E2.80.9385-205">[205]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Founding_of_cities">Founding of cities</span></h3>
<p>Over the course of his conquests, Alexander founded some <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Alexandria_(disambiguation)" title="Alexandria (disambiguation)">twenty cities that bore his name</a>, most of them east of the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livius_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-livius-206">[206]</a></sup> The first, and greatest, was <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> in Egypt, which would become one of the leading Mediterranean cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111-85">[85]</a></sup> The cities' locations reflected trade routes as well as defensive positions. At first, the cities must have been inhospitable, little more than defensive garrisons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111_85-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111-85">[85]</a></sup> Following Alexander's death, many Greeks who had settled there tried to return to Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111_85-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livius_206-1"><a href="#cite_note-livius-206">[206]</a></sup> However, a century or so after Alexander's death, many of the Alexandrias were thriving, with elaborate public buildings and substantial populations that included both Greek and local peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111_85-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenization">Hellenization</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" title="Hellenistic civilization">Hellenistic civilization</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1288" data-file-width="2321" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg/220px-Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg/330px-Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg/440px-Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Alexander's empire was the largest state of its time, covering approximately 5.2 million square km.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Hellenization</i> was coined by the German historian <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gustav_Droysen" title="Johann Gustav Droysen">Johann Gustav Droysen</a> to denote the spread of Greek language, culture, and population into the former Persian empire after Alexander's conquest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen2007xii.E2.80.93xix_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen2007xii.E2.80.93xix-204">[204]</a></sup> That this export took place is undoubted, and can be seen in the great Hellenistic cities of, for instance, <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200756.E2.80.9359_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200756.E2.80.9359-207">[207]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Seleucia" title="Seleucia">Seleucia</a> (south of modern <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> Alexander sought to insert Greek elements into <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Iran" title="Culture of Iran">Persian culture</a> and attempted to hybridize Greek and Persian culture. This culminated in his aspiration to homogenize the populations of Asia and Europe. However, his successors explicitly rejected such policies. Nevertheless, Hellenization occurred throughout the region, accompanied by a distinct and opposite 'Orientalization' of the successor states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200721.2C_56.E2.80.9359_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200721.2C_56.E2.80.9359-209">[209]</a></sup></p>
<p>The core of the Hellenistic culture promulgated by the conquests was essentially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athenian" title="Athenian">Athenian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> The close association of men from across Greece in Alexander's army directly led to the emergence of the largely <a href="/wiki/Attic_Greek" title="Attic Greek">Attic</a>-based "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Koine" title="Koine">koine</a>", or "common" Greek dialect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison197151_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison197151-211">[211]</a></sup> Koine spread throughout the Hellenistic world, becoming the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> of Hellenistic lands and eventually the ancestor of <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">modern Greek</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison197151_211-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison197151-211">[211]</a></sup> Furthermore, <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">town planning</a>, education, local government, and art current in the Hellenistic period were all based on Classical Greek ideals, evolving into distinct new forms commonly grouped as Hellenistic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200756.E2.80.9359_207-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200756.E2.80.9359-207">[207]</a></sup> Aspects of Hellenistic culture were still evident in the traditions of the Byzantine Empire in the mid-15th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gandhara_Buddha_(tnm).jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2894" data-file-width="1746" height="365" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg/220px-Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg/330px-Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg/440px-Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gandhara_Buddha_(tnm).jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Buddha</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhist_art" title="Greco-Buddhist art">Greco-Buddhist style</a>, 1st–2nd century AD, <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a>, ancient India. <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_National_Museum" title="Tokyo National Museum">Tokyo National Museum</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Some of the most pronounced effects of Hellenization can be seen in Afghanistan and India, in the region of the relatively late-rising <a href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_Kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian Kingdom">Greco-Bactrian Kingdom</a> (250 BC-125 BC) (in modern <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Greeks" title="Indo-Greeks">Indo-Greek Kingdom</a> (180 BC - 10 CE) in modern Afghanistan and India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeay2001101.E2.80.939_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeay2001101.E2.80.939-213">[213]</a></sup> There on the newly formed <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> Greek culture apparently hybridized with Indian, and especially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> culture. The resulting <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretism</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhism" title="Greco-Buddhism">Greco-Buddhism</a> heavily influenced the development of Buddhism<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and created a culture of <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhist_art" title="Greco-Buddhist art">Greco-Buddhist art</a>. These Greco-Buddhist kingdoms sent some of the first Buddhist missionaries to <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, and the Mediterranean (<a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhist_monasticism" title="Greco-Buddhist monasticism">Greco-Buddhist monasticism</a>). Some of the first and most influential figurative portrayals of the <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Buddha</a> appeared at this time, perhaps modeled on Greek statues of <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> in the Greco-Buddhist style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeay2001101.E2.80.939_213-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeay2001101.E2.80.939-213">[213]</a></sup> Several Buddhist traditions may have been influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_religion" title="Ancient Greek religion">ancient Greek religion</a>: the concept of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boddhisatvas" title="Boddhisatvas">Boddhisatvas</a> is reminiscent of Greek divine heroes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> and some <a href="/wiki/Mahayana" title="Mahayana">Mahayana</a> <a href="/wiki/Offering_(Buddhism)" title="Offering (Buddhism)">ceremonial practices</a> (burning <a href="/wiki/Incense" title="Incense">incense</a>, gifts of flowers, and food placed on altars) are similar to those practiced by the ancient Greeks; however, similar practices were also observed amongst the native Indic culture. One Greek king, <a href="/wiki/Menander_I" title="Menander I">Menander I</a>, probably became Buddhist, and was immortalized in <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_texts" title="Buddhist texts">Buddhist literature</a> as 'Milinda'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeay2001101.E2.80.939_213-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeay2001101.E2.80.939-213">[213]</a></sup> The process of Hellenization also spurred trade between the east and west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pingree_.281978.29.2C_533.2C_554f._215-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pingree_.281978.29.2C_533.2C_554f.-215">[215]</a></sup> For example, Greek astronomical instruments dating to the 3rd century BC were found in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian" title="Greco-Bactrian">Greco-Bactrian</a> city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ai_Khanoum" title="Ai Khanoum">Ai Khanoum</a> in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> while the Greek concept of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spherical_earth" title="Spherical earth">spherical earth</a> surrounded by the spheres of planets eventually supplanted the long-standing Indian cosmological belief of a disc consisting of four continents grouped around a central mountain (Mount Meru) like the petals of a flower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pingree_.281978.29.2C_533.2C_554f._215-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pingree_.281978.29.2C_533.2C_554f.-215">[215]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hayashi08Aryabhata_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hayashi08Aryabhata-218">[218]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Yavanajataka" title="Yavanajataka">Yavanajataka</a> (lit. Greek astronomical treatise) and <a href="/wiki/Paulisa_Siddhanta" title="Paulisa Siddhanta">Paulisa Siddhanta</a> texts depict the influence of Greek astronomical ideas on Indian astronomy.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_on_Rome">Influence on Rome</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="1711" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg/220px-Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg/330px-Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg/440px-Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This medallion was produced in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Rome" title="Imperial Rome">Imperial Rome</a>, demonstrating the influence of Alexander's memory. <a href="/wiki/Walters_Art_Museum" title="Walters Art Museum">Walters Art Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alexander and his exploits were admired by many Romans, especially generals, who wanted to associate themselves with his achievements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asirvatham_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asirvatham-219">[219]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Polybius" title="Polybius">Polybius</a> began his <i><a href="/wiki/The_Histories_(Polybius)" title="The Histories (Polybius)">Histories</a></i> by reminding Romans of Alexander's achievements, and thereafter Roman leaders saw him as a role model. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pompey_the_Great" title="Pompey the Great">Pompey the Great</a> adopted the epithet "Magnus" and even Alexander's anastole-type haircut, and searched the conquered lands of the east for Alexander's 260-year-old cloak, which he then wore as a sign of greatness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asirvatham_219-1"><a href="#cite_note-Asirvatham-219">[219]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> dedicated a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lysippus" title="Lysippus">Lysippean</a> <a href="/wiki/Equestrian_statue" title="Equestrian statue">equestrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a> statue but replaced Alexander's head with his own, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Octavian" title="Octavian">Octavian</a> visited Alexander's tomb in Alexandria and temporarily changed his seal from a <a href="/wiki/Sphinx" title="Sphinx">sphinx</a> to Alexander's profile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asirvatham_219-2"><a href="#cite_note-Asirvatham-219">[219]</a></sup> The emperor <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a> also admired Alexander, as did <a href="/wiki/Nero" title="Nero">Nero</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caracalla" title="Caracalla">Caracalla</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asirvatham_219-3"><a href="#cite_note-Asirvatham-219">[219]</a></sup> The Macriani, a Roman family that in the person of <a href="/wiki/Macrinus" title="Macrinus">Macrinus</a> briefly ascended to the imperial throne, kept images of Alexander on their persons, either on jewelry, or embroidered into their clothes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolt20033_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolt20033-220">[220]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:203px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Demetrius_I_of_Bactria.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="191" data-file-width="201" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a9/Demetrius_I_of_Bactria.jpg" width="201"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Demetrius_I_of_Bactria.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian" title="Greco-Bactrian">Greco-Bactrian</a> king <a href="/wiki/Demetrius_I_of_Bactria" title="Demetrius I of Bactria">Demetrius</a> (reigned c. 200–180 BC), wearing an elephant scalp, took over Alexander's legacy in the east by again invading <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, and establishing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Greek_kingdom" title="Indo-Greek kingdom">Indo-Greek kingdom</a> (180 BC–10 AD).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_coronation_of_Alexander.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="737" data-file-width="800" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/The_coronation_of_Alexander.jpg/220px-The_coronation_of_Alexander.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/The_coronation_of_Alexander.jpg/330px-The_coronation_of_Alexander.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/The_coronation_of_Alexander.jpg/440px-The_coronation_of_Alexander.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_coronation_of_Alexander.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The coronation of Alexander depicted in medieval European style in the 15th century romance <i>The History of Alexander's Battles</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On the other hand, some Roman writers, particularly Republican figures, used Alexander as a cautionary tale of how <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autocratic" title="Autocratic">autocratic</a> tendencies can be kept in check by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_republic" title="Roman republic">republican</a> values.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asirvatham2_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asirvatham2-221">[221]</a></sup> Alexander was used by these writers as an example of ruler values such as <i>amicita</i> (friendship) and <i>clementia</i> (clemency), but also <i>iracundia</i> (anger) and <i>cupiditas gloriae</i> (over-desire for glory).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asirvatham2_221-1"><a href="#cite_note-Asirvatham2-221">[221]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legend">Legend</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great_in_legend" title="Alexander the Great in legend">Alexander the Great in legend</a></div>
<p>Legendary accounts surround the life of Alexander the Great, many deriving from his own lifetime, probably encouraged by Alexander himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010187_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010187-222">[222]</a></sup> His court historian Callisthenes portrayed the sea in <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a> as drawing back from him in proskynesis. Writing shortly after Alexander's death, another participant, <a href="/wiki/Onesicritus" title="Onesicritus">Onesicritus</a>, invented a <a href="/wiki/Courtship" title="Courtship">tryst</a> between Alexander and <a href="/wiki/Thalestris" title="Thalestris">Thalestris</a>, queen of the mythical <a href="/wiki/Amazons" title="Amazons">Amazons</a>. When Onesicritus read this passage to his patron, Alexander's general and later King <a href="/wiki/Lysimachus" title="Lysimachus">Lysimachus</a> reportedly quipped, "I wonder where I was at the time."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA46_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA46-223">[223]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the first centuries after Alexander's death, probably in Alexandria, a quantity of the legendary material coalesced into a text known as the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_Romance" title="Alexander Romance">Alexander Romance</a></i>, later falsely ascribed to Callisthenes and therefore known as <i>Pseudo-Callisthenes</i>. This text underwent numerous expansions and revisions throughout Antiquity and the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> containing many dubious stories,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010187_222-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010187-222">[222]</a></sup> and was translated into numerous languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010117_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010117-225">[225]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_ancient_and_modern_culture">In ancient and modern culture</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Cultural depictions of Alexander the Great">Cultural depictions of Alexander the Great</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great_in_the_Quran" title="Alexander the Great in the Quran">Alexander the Great in the Quran</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_(cropped).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="884" data-file-width="1225" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_%28cropped%29.JPG/220px-Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_%28cropped%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_%28cropped%29.JPG/330px-Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_%28cropped%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_%28cropped%29.JPG/440px-Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_%28cropped%29.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_(cropped).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Alexander the Great depicted in a 14th-century Byzantine manuscript</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eskandar_fighting_the_enemy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="471" height="327" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Eskandar_fighting_the_enemy.jpg/220px-Eskandar_fighting_the_enemy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Eskandar_fighting_the_enemy.jpg/330px-Eskandar_fighting_the_enemy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Eskandar_fighting_the_enemy.jpg/440px-Eskandar_fighting_the_enemy.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eskandar_fighting_the_enemy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Alexander the Great depicted in a 15th-century <a href="/wiki/Persian_miniature" title="Persian miniature">Persian miniature</a> painting.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alexander the Great's accomplishments and legacy have been depicted in many cultures. Alexander has figured in both high and popular culture beginning in his own era to the present day. The <i>Alexander Romance</i>, in particular, has had a significant impact on portrayals of Alexander in later cultures, from Persian to medieval European to modern Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010117_225-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010117-225">[225]</a></sup></p>
<p>Alexander features prominently in modern Greek folklore, more so than any other ancient figure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fermor_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fermor-226">[226]</a></sup> The colloquial form of his name in modern Greek ("O Megalexandros") is a household name, and he is the only ancient hero to appear in the <a href="/wiki/Karagiozis" title="Karagiozis">Karagiozis</a> shadow play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fermor_226-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fermor-226">[226]</a></sup> One well-known fable among Greek seamen involves a solitary <a href="/wiki/Mermaid" title="Mermaid">mermaid</a> who would grasp a ship's prow during a storm and ask the captain "Is King Alexander alive?" The correct answer is "He is alive and well and rules the world!" causing the mermaid to vanish and the sea to calm. Any other answer would cause the mermaid to turn into a raging <a href="/wiki/Gorgon" title="Gorgon">Gorgon</a> who would drag the ship to the bottom of the sea, all hands aboard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fermor_226-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fermor-226">[226]</a></sup></p>
<p>In pre-Islamic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Persian_language" title="Middle Persian language">Middle Persian</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zoroastrian" title="Zoroastrian">Zoroastrian</a>) literature, Alexander is referred to by the epithet <i>gujastak</i>, meaning "accursed", and is accused of destroying temples and burning the sacred texts of Zoroastrianism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> In Islamic Iran, under the influence of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_Romance" title="Alexander Romance">Alexander Romance</a></i> (in <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="fa" xml:lang="fa">اسکندرنامه</span>‎‎ <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iskandarnamah" title="Iskandarnamah">Iskandarnamah</a></i>), a more positive portrayal of Alexander emerges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120-228">[228]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Firdausi" title="Firdausi">Firdausi</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Shahnameh" title="Shahnameh">Shahnameh</a></i> ("The Book of Kings") includes Alexander in a line of legitimate Iranian <a href="/wiki/Shah" title="Shah">shahs</a>, a mythical figure who explored the far reaches of the world in search of the <a href="/wiki/Fountain_of_Youth" title="Fountain of Youth">Fountain of Youth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> Later Persian writers associate him with philosophy, portraying him at a symposium with figures such as <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> and Aristotle, in search of immortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120_228-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120-228">[228]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a> version of the <i>Alexander Romance</i> portrays him as an ideal Christian world conqueror who prayed to "the one true God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120_228-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120-228">[228]</a></sup> In Egypt, Alexander was portrayed as the son of <a href="/wiki/Nectanebo_II" title="Nectanebo II">Nectanebo II</a>, the last <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">pharaoh</a> before the Persian conquest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010122_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010122-230">[230]</a></sup> His defeat of Darius was depicted as Egypt's salvation, "proving" Egypt was still ruled by an Egyptian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120_228-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120-228">[228]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a>, Alexander was shown the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Daniel" title="Book of Daniel">Book of Daniel</a> when he entered Jerusalem, which described a mighty Greek king who would conquer the Persian Empire. This is cited as a reason for sparing Jerusalem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup></p>
<p>The figure of <a href="/wiki/Dhul-Qarnayn" title="Dhul-Qarnayn">Dhul-Qarnayn</a> (literally "the Two-Horned One") mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> is believed by some scholars to represent Alexander, due to parallels with the <i>Alexander Romance</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120_228-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120-228">[228]</a></sup> In this tradition, he was a heroic figure who built a wall to defend against the nations of <a href="/wiki/Gog_and_Magog" title="Gog and Magog">Gog and Magog</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010122_230-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010122-230">[230]</a></sup> He then travelled the known world in search of the Water of Life and Immortality, eventually becoming a prophet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010122_230-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010122-230">[230]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>, the name "Sikandar", derived from Persian, denotes a rising young talent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">medieval Europe</a> he was made a member of the <a href="/wiki/Nine_Worthies" title="Nine Worthies">Nine Worthies</a>, a group of heroes who encapsulated all the ideal qualities of <a href="/wiki/Chivalry" title="Chivalry">chivalry</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great_in_historiography" title="Alexander the Great in historiography">Alexander the Great in historiography</a></div>
<p>Apart from a few inscriptions and fragments, texts written by people who actually knew Alexander or who gathered information from men who served with Alexander were all lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186-16">[16]</a></sup> Contemporaries who wrote accounts of his life included Alexander's campaign historian Callisthenes; Alexander's generals Ptolemy and <a href="/wiki/Nearchus" title="Nearchus">Nearchus</a>; <a href="/wiki/Aristobulus_of_Cassandreia" title="Aristobulus of Cassandreia">Aristobulus</a>, a junior officer on the campaigns; and Onesicritus, Alexander's chief helmsman. Their works are lost, but later works based on these <a href="/wiki/Primary_source" title="Primary source">original sources</a> have survived. The earliest of these is <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a> (1st century BC), followed by Quintus Curtius Rufus (mid-to-late 1st century AD), Arrian (1st to 2nd century AD), the biographer Plutarch (1st to 2nd century AD), and finally <a href="/wiki/Justin_(historian)" title="Justin (historian)">Justin</a>, whose work dated as late as the 4th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186_16-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186-16">[16]</a></sup> Of these, Arrian is generally considered the most reliable, given that he used Ptolemy and Aristobulus as his sources, closely followed by Diodorus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186_16-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186-16">[16]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Annotations">Annotations</span></h2>
<div style="">
<ol class="references" style="list-style-type:upper-alpha;">
<li id="cnote_a"><b><a href="#ref_a_1">^</a></b> Macedon was an Ancient Greek polity. The Macedonians were a Hellenic (Greek) tribe. Historiography and scholarship agree that Alexander the Great was Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macedonians_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-Macedonians-233">[233]</a></sup></li>
<li id="cnote_b"><b><a href="#ref_b_1">^</a></b> By the time of his death, he had conquered the entire <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Persian Empire</a>, adding it to Macedon's European territories; according to some modern writers, this was most of the world then known to the ancient Greeks (the '<a href="/wiki/Ecumene#Ancient_world" title="Ecumene">Ecumene</a>').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDanforth199738.2C_49.2C_167_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDanforth199738.2C_49.2C_167-234">[234]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoneman20042_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoneman20042-235">[235]</a></sup> An approximate view of the world known to Alexander can be seen in <a href="/wiki/Hecataeus_of_Miletus" title="Hecataeus of Miletus">Hecataeus of Miletus</a>'s map; see <a href="/wiki/File:Hecataeus_world_map-en.svg" title="File:Hecataeus world map-en.svg">Hecataeus world map</a>.</li>
<li id="cnote_c"><b><a href="#ref_c_1">^</a></b> For instance, <a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">Hannibal</a> supposedly ranked Alexander as the greatest general;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldsworthy2003327.E2.80.9328_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldsworthy2003327.E2.80.9328-236">[236]</a></sup> Julius Caesar wept on seeing a statue of Alexander, since he had achieved so little by the same age;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plutarch.2C_Caesar.2C_11_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plutarch.2C_Caesar.2C_11-237">[237]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a> consciously posed as the 'new Alexander';<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolland2003176.E2.80.9383_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolland2003176.E2.80.9383-238">[238]</a></sup> the young <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_Bonaparte" title="Napoleon Bonaparte">Napoleon Bonaparte</a> also encouraged comparisons with Alexander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnett199745_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnett199745-239">[239]</a></sup></li>
<li id="cnote_d"><b><a href="#ref_d_1">^</a></b> The name <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">Ἀλέξανδρος</span> derives from the Greek verb <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">ἀλέξω</span> (<i>alexō</i>) "ward off, avert, defend"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.perseus.tufts.edu.2Fhopper.2Ftext.3Fdoc.3DPerseus.253Atext.253A1999.04.0057.253Aentry.253Da.2529le.252Fcw_IV.2C_57.5D_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.perseus.tufts.edu.2Fhopper.2Ftext.3Fdoc.3DPerseus.253Atext.253A1999.04.0057.253Aentry.253Da.2529le.252Fcw_IV.2C_57.5D-240">[240]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddellScott1940_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddellScott1940-241">[241]</a></sup> and <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">ἀνδρ-</span> (<i>andr-</i>), the stem of <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">ἀνήρ</span> (<i>anēr</i>) "man",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.perseus.tufts.edu.2Fhopper.2Ftext.3Fdoc.3DPerseus.253Atext.253A1999.04.0057.253Aentry.253Da.2529nh.252Fr_IV.2C_57.5D_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.perseus.tufts.edu.2Fhopper.2Ftext.3Fdoc.3DPerseus.253Atext.253A1999.04.0057.253Aentry.253Da.2529nh.252Fr_IV.2C_57.5D-242">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddellScott1940_241-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddellScott1940-241">[241]</a></sup> and means "protector of men."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup></li>
<li id="cnote_e"><b><a href="#ref_e_1">^</a></b> There have been, since the time, many suspicions that Pausanias was actually hired to murder Philip. Suspicion has fallen upon Alexander, Olympias and even the newly crowned Persian Emperor, Darius III. All three of these people had motive to have Philip murdered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFox198072.E2.80.9373_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFox198072.E2.80.9373-244">[244]</a></sup></li>
<li id="cnote_f"><b><a href="#ref_f_1">^</a></b> However, <a href="/wiki/Arrian" title="Arrian">Arrian</a>, who used <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_I_Soter" title="Ptolemy I Soter">Ptolemy</a> as a source, said that Alexander crossed with more than 5,000 horse and 30,000 foot; <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus</a> quoted the same totals, but listed 5,100 horse and 32,000 foot. Diodorus also referred to an advance force already present in Asia, which <a href="/wiki/Polyaenus" title="Polyaenus">Polyaenus</a>, in his Stratagems of War (5.44.4), said numbered 10,000 men.</li>
</ol>
</div>
